SUCCESSFUL. FAIR
IT SANTA CRUZ
Many Handsome Exhib
its in Armory Hall.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA CRUZ, Oct. 17.â The Fourteenth
District Agricultural Association Fair
opened this evening at the armory. Thid
building has been transformed by the
wonderful display of the products of this
county.
The exhibits are many and are in all
departments. The first exhibit which at
tracts attention is that of the agricultural,
horticultural and viticultural department.
The exhibit occupies the center space of
the building. The long tables are heaped
high with pyramids of bright-hued ap
ples, and pears and plates of grapes are
relieved by the many, hued leaves of the
grape vine.
Dried fruits are exhibited In the great
est quantities, as are wine, nuts and the
products of the farm.
A delightful spot is the corner reserved
for the lloral exhibit. The local florists
have displayed the finest plants from their
hothouses, while the wealth of floral
bloom from many gardens cannot be
duplicated at any lair in the State.
The line arts department is in a room
off the main building, and the walls are
hidden from view by the display of oil
and water color paintings and crayon
work. In this room are also the displays
of costly hand-painted china.
The department reserved for fancy work
and needlework occupies threo rooms ou
the second floor. ;--.-.;
The officers of the association are:
Henry K. Dakm, president; ' Oscar L.
Gordon, secretary; directorsâ H. R. Da
kin, F. A. Hihn. D. M. Locke. I. H. Tuttle.
W. li. Galbraith, W. S. Rodgers. Duncan
McPherson and L. P. Lauretzen.

lows: Livestock, F. A. Hihn; poultry,
Philip Hvn.-s: field products, l". W.
Thompson; dairy products, D. D. Wilder;
horticulture, L. P. Lauretzen; viticulture,
W. W. Waterman; canned fruit, preserves,
pfckles, Mrs. Susie Harris; preserved
meats, W. H. Galbraith; lumber, F. O.

ing, Frank L. Heath; embroidery and

COAL MINERS STRIKE.
Two Thousand Leave Their Posts at
Spring' Valley, Illinois.-
SPRING VALLEY, 111.. Oct. 17.-State
President J. M. Hunter arrived here last
night on the call of the executive com
mittee of this district and to-day called a
strike of the 2000 miners employed by the
Spring Valley Coal Company. The men
all struck and the supply of coai from
this point will drop 5000 tons daily until a
settlement is made.
The difficulty which brought on the
strike is alleged to have been the refusal
of General Manager Dalzell to stop union
dues at the company's office.
Dewey's Visit to Atlanta.
WASHINGTON, Oct. 17.â A committee
representing the citizens of Atlanta called
upon Admiral Dewey to-day to arrange
the details of his trip to Atlanta. It was
decided that, In company with Lieutenant
Brumby, who is to be presented with a
sword- by the city of Atlanta, and a citi
zens' committee, the admiral will leave
Washington next Monday night, arriving
at Atlanta Tuesday evening. He will re
view the military display in Atlanta, take
part in the presentation of the sword and
attend the dinner to be given . by the
Capitol Club. '
ACCIDENTALLY
SHOT HIMSELF
Major Upham Killed at
Santa Monica.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LOS ANGELES, Oct. 17.-Major Frank
Kidder Upham, treasurer, acting quarter
master and commissary of subsistence at
the Soldiers' Home at Santa Monica, ac
cidentally shot and killed himself at the
home this morning. He had been notified
that the money for the monthly pay roll
had arrived at the depot. He went to his
desk to take out his revolver, lt being his
custom to arm himself while bringing the
money from the depot.
As he took the revolver from its case
the hammer caught on the edge of a table
and one cartridge was discharged, the
hall entering his neck under the chin and
passing out at the base of the brain. He
died instantly. TXaBI
Major Upham served in the Second Cali
fornia Infantry from I*o4 until ISB7 and
then entered the regular army and was
mustered out February _. 1892, as a cap
tain. He was elected to his present posi
tion at the home April 20, 1835. He leaves
a widow, who is at the home, two daugh
i ters, Ethel and Edith, who are at board
' ing school In this city, and two sons, one
| F. B. I'pham, an ensign on the Olympia
j who was with Dewey at Manila, and John
Upham, a cadet at West Point.
Ensign Upham Is now on his way to
: visit his parents. He is not yet informed
of his father's death.
BANK PRESIDENT SUES.
Alleges That Stock Known to Be
Worthless Was Sold Him.
LOS ANGELES, Oct. 17.â Charles R.
Mayers has brought suit in the United
States District Court against Theodore P.
Gordon to recover $4287 and Interest.
Mayers Is president of the First National
Bank of Columbus. Ohio. The defendant
was formerly cashier of the bank, but Is
now a resident of Pasadena .
The complaint alleges that the plaintiff
purchased the bank largely on the show
ing of assets made and that after the
purchase it was found that much of the
paper was worthless and the bookkeeper
had absconded. Among the worthless pa
per represented in the cashier's statement
as good were notes signed by L. R. Doty,
an insolvent. It ls alleged that Gordon
knew of Doty's Insolvent condition, but
represented. his notes as good with a de
sire and intent to defraud. The plaintiff
alleges that he was damaged by the al
leged fraud to the extent of $4287 and In
terest at 5 per cent. The defendant de
nies all the allegations and will vigor
ously contest the case.

Kicked by a Horse.
SAN ANSELMO, Oct. 17.-David, the
16-year-old son of James I. Taylor, a
brother of Sheriff Taylor, was kicked In
the jaw and seriously injured by. a horse
yesterday. The boy slapped the horse on
the hip as it entered the barn door with
out any warning, and frightened at the
blow, ' the horse kicked him. He was
rendered senseless, an ; examination by Dr
W. J. /Wickman showing that his upper
jaw was fractured, several teeth knocked
out and a cut made in the lower Ilp and
chin requiring twenty stitches.

SOLDIERS SHOT
NOT WISELY HOT
FAR TOO WELL
Surplus Enthusiasm
of Montana Men.
Â« '.
CELEBRATING MUSTER OUT
â¦
A BECALMED SCHOONER UNDER
FIRE AND HIT FOUR TIMES.
â¦ . â .
Captain Joseph D. Leitch Has Been
Made an Aid to General
'Shafterâ A Sword for
L y Wholly.
There came a sequel yesterday to the
jollification of the Montana men during
their last night of service. Monday even
ing the Montana camp was a lively scene.
The Kansas men had come up to have
a social with the Montana men, and part
of the entertainment, furnished the guests
was the firing of rifles. The regiment had
turned in all arms, but the Mausers and
Remingtons captured from the Spaniards
and Filipinos belonged to the men, and
they had plenty of ammunition. The
firing continued for over an hour, and
then most of the shooters turned their
attention to other forms of amusement.
Yesterday morning the master of the
schooner Ida A appeared at headquarters
and announced that while becalmed off
the Presidio his boat was hit. four times
by the bullets from the enthusiastic Mon
tanans and that for some time he and his
crew were in great danger of being shot.
The bullets were hitting the water all
around the: . and as there was no wind
they could not get out of range. They
were perhaps two miles from the camp
on the hill where the men were shooting,
and it was no use for them to call out.
All they could do was to trust to luck
that they would drift out of range or
that the firing would cease before any
of them were hit. As it was, three shots
struck the vessel and one went through
her sail.
An Investigation was Immediately
ordered, but it had little result. The Mon
tana men were mustered out yesterday
and there was no time to make a thor
ough investigation of the affair, besides
no one would admit that he fired any
shots, or that he saw any one else do so,
or even that any shots had been fired
from the Montana camp. It was pointed
out that the men of the Montana regi
ment had turned in all their arms and
therefore had nothing to shoot with, but
it was not with Government arms they
expressed their enthusiasm. it was fur
ther declared that most of the shooting
came from the Kansas camp, but it is not
probable that more than half a dozen of
the shots were tired from there. At any
rate, the investigation has developed
nothing.
Later in the day another complaint
came in from Harbor View. One of the
bullets had gone through a bedroom, and
the occupant wanted to be sure there
would be no repetition. Bullets were also
heard to whistle over the Presidio build
ings, but at the time it was not supposed
that any one was shooting ball cartridges.
Those who heard the men thought tney
were shooting blanks, and while it was
believed to be a little off color even with
blanks, from a military standpoint, still
none of the volunteer officers saw fit to
stop it. There is no guard from the post
garrison over the volunteer camps, so no
report of the shooting was turned in to
headquarters. '
Colonel Freeman, commandant of the
post, Issued an order yesterday forbidding
the discharge of firearms in any of the
camps. It has been a practice of the reg
iments to be mustered out to celebrate
their last night in service with some such
demonstration, but heretofore blank
cartridges have been used. Now the
practice will have to stop altogether.
Captain J. D. Leitch was relieved from
duty as an adjutant of the Presidio yes
terday and he was ordered to duty as an
aid to General Shafter. He has received
no orders from Washington assigning him
to the position, but it is not Improbable !
that he will he ordered away from this I
department. His place as post adjutant
has been taken by Captain James 11. Fri
er. Seventeenth Infantry.
Monday evening the officers of the Pre
sidio and those of the Twenty-eighth
Regiment called upon General Shafter to
pay their respects and to congrati 'ate
him upon his retention In the vo' \,.'teer
service and as commander of this depart
ment. The band of the Twenty-eighth
Regiment was with the officers and It ser
enaded the general. There were nearly
seventy officers in attendance.

of Captain W. N. Hughes, Thirteenth In
fantry, and Lieutenants W. N. Hughes
Wilson. Pasco.' and Kinzie have been as
signed to the transport Manuense with
two companies of the Thirty-first 'under
command of Lieutenant Colonel Hayes
The Montana regiment was mustered
out yesterday, each man receiving in the
neighborhood of $100 in pay and allow
ances. The regiment will start east on a
special train this evening, the train to
leave from the 9 o'clock boat from this
side. The bulk of the regiment will go
over on the 8 o'clock boat, and the 9
o'clock will be the last that will catch :+>.
train. --....â 
The men of the Washington regiment
presented Colonel Wholly with a beautiful
sword yesterday. It was the finest the
men could buy, and every man in the regi
ment contributed his share to the pur
chase price. On the blade is the colonel's
name and the fact that it was a token of
esteem from the enlisted men whom he
commanded.
It was payday ln the Kansas regiment
yesterday, but one month's money was all
the men received. They will be mustered
out November 3.
The detachment from Battery N, Third
Artillery, now stationed at Benicia Bar
racks, will be relieved by a detachment*
from Battery E of the same regiment,
which will be sent from Fort Mason. The
detachment from Battery N will return to
its company's station, Alcatraz Island.

MORTGAGE DEFAULTED.
Wells, Fargo & Co. Win a Suit to
Foreclose on Property in This
City and Suburban Towns.
A default was entered in the United
States Circuit Court yesterday on motion
of E. S. Pillsbury. attorney for the plain
tiff, in the suit of Wells, Fargo & Co. to
foreclose a mortgage made to secure a
promissory note for $45,528 52, executed on
August 31. 1593. The defendants are Verba
Buena Mutual Building and Loan Associa
tion, James B. Brooks, Eveline M. Graves
and others. The property affected con
sists of a piece of land on Jersey street,
east of Douglass, lots on San Bruno ave
nue and Lux avenue in this city, lots on
Spruce and Grand avenues in South San
Francisco, and lots in the towns of Berke
ley and Alameda.
The case was referred to United States
Commissioner Heacock to take testimony
as to the amount due on the note, between
$5000 and $6000 having been paid.

LUMBER DRIFTS ASHORE.
Residents of Bolinas Profit by the
Aberdeen's Loss.
BOLINAS. Oct. About 100,000 feet of
pine lumber which was washed from the
deck of the steamer Aberdeen last Tues
day while she was on her way from Port
land, Or., to San Francisco, floated into
Bolinas Bay last Friday. There was so
much of the lumber that for a half mile
from shore the ocean appeared yellow.
The Incident created a good deal of ex
citement, and for a while residents were
busily engaged in removing the lumber
from the. beach to their homes. All the
lumber now reposes in piles and .stacks
on the various ranches in the vicinity,
where it will lie used for firewood, as it
is cut into too large scantlings to be used
iii local building operations
It was on last Tuesday that the Aber
deen. Captain Pedersen, ; encountered a
fierce northwester off Point Reyes. The
lashings of the lumber which formed her
cargo broke loose and some of the lumber
getting caught in the rigging the main
mast of the steamer was carried away
Water poured into the engine-room and
the cabin and for a few minutes it was
feared that the fires would be extin
guished The vessel was turned bows on
and weathered the storm. She reached
Lombard-street wharf. San Francisco,
with about 500.000 feet of lumber and was
sent to Oakland Creek after discharging.

NAPA, Oct. 17.â Teachers' Institute

GENERAL GREEN'S VIEWS.
IF irrigation has a father in California that person
is General Will S. Green, whose views The Call
* published yesterday. We are of the opinion that
an examination of his position will show him that the
water storage movement is one solution of the very
difficulties which he presents. The present laws for
the enjoyment and distribution of water relate to the
normal flow of the streams and originate in riparian
rights. The application of the common law of ri
parian ownership has undergone certain statutory
modifications, and the water is subject to judicial
distribution, according to its use, but we need not
enter now into these details. They all concern the
normal flow and not the floods.
With the storage of flood waters we approach an
other view of the law of distribution that is not af
fected by riparian ownership nor subject to any of the
limitations now imposed.
As General Green is aware, many ditch and canal
companies which have sold permanent water rights that
go with the land have oversold. That is to say, their
tale of water has been measured not by the minimum
normal flow nor by the average, but by the maxi
mumâby that flow that fills the banks. The result
is that when dry years come and the flow is at the
minimum they cannot deliver the water they have
sold as a permanent right. While one may severely
impeach their lack of foresight, or may cail by a
harsher name their sale of water they cannot be sure
of possessing, hard judgment on them does not re
lieve the irrigator. He needs the water, and if the
ditch-owner can find it in a reservoir where flood
water is stored he can be compelled to get it and
fill his tracts.
Aside from this the distribution of flood water will
be independent of the difficulties now existing and so
well described by General Green. We hope to see
bim in the coming convention, for he has much to
teach and will meet there many who have much to
learn.
If Sir Thomas Lipton succeeds now in his boast
to "lift" the America challenge cup he will have to
be something more of a heavy-weight than he aspires
to be.
PATROL WAGON DRIVERS.
BY the recent order of the Board of Supervisors
\ discharging all patrol drivers now in the em
ploy of the city and placing the selection and
appointment of employes of that branch of the mu
nicipal service in the hands of the Police Department
a true reform was effected in the city administration.
It is therefore inevitable there should be opposition
to it. The bosses who profited by the old system
and who used the positions of patrol drivers as a part
of the spoils of oifice for distribution among their
follo'wcvs arc of course eager to set the reform aside
and return to- the old way. A movement has been
made in the Board of Supervisors to conform to their
wishes to a partial extent at least, and there is now
under consideration a motion to defer the date when
the reform is to go into effect from November I to
December 15.
The issue in the case is a simple one. If the patrol
drivers be placed under control of the Police Depart
ment there will be a clear responsibility in that de-
partment for the selection of proper men for the ser
vice. At present the places are given out at the dic
tation of ward bosses and are designed as rewards for
partisan activity rather than for the work of the
municipality. The patrol drivers are of course in
close relations with the police force and are virtually
a part of that force. It is therefore fitting they should
be under the control of that department.
Nor is there any good reason why the change hi
the system of appointment should be delayed. When
a thing is worth doing it is well to do it promptly. A
postponement now would be a precedent for another
postponement later on. The reform is a good one.
It io line with the progress which) has been made
in eliminating boss control from our politics. It is
oppo c ed only by Kelly and Crimmins and men of
that kind. It will work a marked improvement in
the patrol service and be also a help and an advan
tage to th.c Police Department in the performance of
the work that is required of it. It should therefore be
tamed as it standi.
MERCHANT MARINE SUBSIDIES.
COMMISSIONER of Navigation Chamber
lain has made an important contribution
to the discussion of the merchant marine
bill by an official report showing the amount
of subsidies paid to their commercial marine by the
greater nations of the globe. The showing is by no
means flattering to the United States', inasmuch as it
reveals that we are far behind our rivals in the race
for commercial supremacy.
Subsidies to shipping are granted for various pur
j poses, such as mail contracts, sen-ice in time of war,
: retainers to seamen, for the training of apprentices
and for fishing bounties. In none of these purposes
; does the United States offer such inducements as are
â  offered elsewhere. Taking the payments as a whole
! it is noted the United States stands far down on the
list, paying only $1,038,141 for ocean mails carried
in American vessels. France, which is making
strenuous efforts to build up a strong merchant fleet,
stands at the head of the list, with payments under
I various headings aggregating 532,242. Great Brit
] am comes next with $5,762,762 paid to ship-owners
and seamen as the contribution of the Government
toward maintaining British supremacy in ocean com
merce. Japan comes third with payments aggregat
ing $3492,107. These are amounts paid by other na
! tions: Italy, $2,185,266; Germany, $1,894,620; Aus
tria-Hungary, $1,724,249; Spain, $1,629,927; Russia,
I $1,168,187.
With that official record before them of what other
nations are doing to promote commerce it is certainly
to be expected that members of Congress will pass
a shipping subsidy bill at the coming session. Our
export and import trade is enormous, but it is car
ried almost wholly in foreign vessels. Senator Frye
estimates we pay $100,000 a day to foreign ship
owners for transporting our commerce, and the drain
makes a heavy reduction annually from our profits.
We have been liberal in providing for the up
building of our industries on land, and the results
have been so remarkable as to constitute one of the
marvels of the century. We are now able to manu
facture not only for our own needs but vast quantities
of goods for foreign markets. A similar policy of
protection and promotion carried out with respect to
our shipping would eventually enable American en
terprise to be as conspicuous on the seas as it is now
on land.
The issue was submitted to the people at the last
Presidential election. The Republican party at that
time took strong ground in favor of an American
merchant marine and gave an emphatic pledge to the
people to provide for its augmentation and expansion.
That pledge has not yet been fulfilled, and now an
other Presidential election is approaching. Under
such circumstances it is clearly the duty of Congress
to provide for our mercantile marine at the coming
session.
Since Mayor Phelan has assumed the dictatorship
of a "new Democracy" it would be within the realm
of Ids arrogated power to force some of his nomi
nees to bear the yoke quietly. Assessor Dodge in
sists that he is a party in himself, and the other
candidates must hustle for themselves. The creed
of the "new Democrats" appears to be everybody for
hints
CAMPAIGN BANNERS.
j AMOTION was made at "the meeting of the
/ \ Board of Supervisors on Monday evening to
i so amend the order forbidding the stretching
of campaign banners across the streets as to permit
thai nuisance on all streets except Market and
Kearny. Th inks to Supervisor Holland the board
was saved from hasty and inconsiderate action on the
subject and the motion vent over for reconsideration
at the next meeting.
The proposed amendment has nothing to justify it.
Either the promiscuous swinging of unsightly
stretches of canvas over our thoroughfares is a nui
sance or it rs not. If it be a nuisance why permit it
in any part of the city? If it be not a nuisance why
prohibit it on Market and Kearny? In what respect
are the people who pass along those streets entitled
to mofe consideration from the municipal authorities
than the people who pa- on other streets?
The order prohibiting that form of public nuisance
has been well received and cordially approved by the
community. There is no objection anywhere to the
artistic decoration of the streets with banners which
are in themselves bright and fair to look upon and
j which have a festal significance. Campaign banners,
fever, are anything rather than artistic. They are
simply huge advertising posters displaying in lurid
letters the name of some aspirant for office. They
mar the appearance of the streets and in many in
stances are as offensive to the mind as to the eye.
There is no reason why the streets should be given
up to that kind of advertising. Candidates for office
have many means by which to parade their names
and their virtues before the voters without granting
them the privilege of turning the streets into a pano
rama of flapping canvas with their names displayed.
Before the subject comes up for reconsideration the
Supervisors will have an opportunity to learn some
thing of public sentiment on'the matter. The political
banner stretched across the street is a serious nuisance
in the eyes of the great mass of the community.
There is a strong sentiment in favor of suppressing it,
and a grave mistake will be made if the recent well
approved order be rescinded or weakened by amend
ment or alteration.
H. H. Kohlsaat, the able editor of the Chicago
Times-Herald, is deservedly distinguished for ability
in many fields, but when the Chronicle framed his
picture on Monday in boxing gloves, championship
belts and other insignia of the prize ring he was
clothed with a pugilistic dignity to which he does not
aspire. lie has a fighting editor of his own.
MR. BRYAN'S LECTURE RATES.
W X BRYAN, according to all reports, has been
/ \ making a good deal of hay out of the political
* * sunshine which has been glowing so brightly
and warmly of late in the Mississippi Valley. A
special correspondent of the Philadelphia Record
quotes a Cabinet official as saying that for his speech
at Canton, 111., the silver aspirant for the Presidency
was paid $600, which is surely a good reward for a
two-hours' talk.
When asked by whom the money was subscribed
the official is reported to have said: "By the citizens
of the town and the people interested in the State
Fair which is in progress this week. Of course Bryan
is an attraction, and he brought several thousand peo
ple to town who would not have come otherwise.
Thus the subscribers had an opportunity to get their
money back. I assume it is all right for Mr. Bryan
to make money this way. Still, isn't it rather undig
nified for a man who poses as the leader of the
Democracy and as a prospective candidate for the
Presidency to accept money for his 'work of educat
ing the people?' " -
While the Record correspondent does not give
the name of the Cabinet official, the statement
TIIE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, OCTOBER 18, 1899.
; regarding the fee paid to Bryan for the Canton speech
j has been confirmed by reports from other sources.
j Whether it be dignified in a candidate for the Presi
dency to charge for addressing the people depends
i upon the kind of man who is posing as a candidate.
j It would be undignified in Admiral Dewey to charge
j for his appearance at any particular city, and it would
I be undignified for Cleveland or Harrison to seek to
j make money by such means. Bryan, however, is a
character unique in our politics. Stumping the coun
try at State fairs for coin is not undignified in him
because it comports well with all other of his per
formances. He has a right to lecture for coin and
it the people are willing to pay his price there is no
reason why he should not make a living that way.
It is a part of his habitual practice to serve the people
"for the money that is in it and not for the honor."

the campaign at Santiago Admiral Sampson prob
ably forgets that he had o only a long distance view
of the situation. And General Shafter adds, with a
touch of sarcasm, that in affairs warlike Admiral
Sampson has not yet won the honor of being a good
judge.
WHILE a considerable number of newspapers
/ have kept out of their columns all reports of
the brilliant successes achieved by the Mar
coni system of wireless telegraphy, it is to be noted
that the more influential journals in the East have
been superior to such petty jealousies and have given
to the work done for The Call and the Herald by
Marconi the most outspoken and cordial praise.
The Philadelphia Ledger, for example, says: "Al
though the yacht races have been inconclusive, the
Marconi system of wireless telegraphy has proved
beyond any question its practicability and value. It
has been employed under rather unfavorable condi
tions â that is to say, with the sending station on a
boat itching about in a rough sea and during foggy
weather â at all times with complete success. Not
a single hitch has occurred, and the number of mes
sages sent and received has far exceeded the ordin
ary demands upon a commercial line. The special
value of the bulletins sent from the Ponce to the
shore lines is that they record from the scene of the
race itself the events as they happen. The bulletins
sent from shore stations are unreliable estimates of
the positions of far distant yachts, which at times can
not be distinguished, and the messages sent from
other boats following the races are necessarily de
layed a long time before they reach land lines or
cables. Wireless telegraphy, therefore, has a field of
its own in reporting events at sea, and the uses of
the system are quite obvious."
The people of San Francisco have had a con
vincing proof that the estimate put upon the Mar
coni service by the Ledger, although high, is not in
any way exaggerated. The bulletins from the races
have been as full as the occasion called for, and, as
the Ledger says, they have in quantity of words ex
ceeded the ordinary demands upon a commercial
wire. Persons in this city watching The Call bulle
tins have kept pace almost with the yachts as they
sailed, the news of each incident of the race being
posted here within two or three minutes after it
occurred.
Wireless telegraphy has, in fact, been accom
plished. It cannot be ignored. Sooner or later the
very journals that now pass its marvels over without
a word of information to their readers will have to
take up the story and tell it. This yacht race will be
memorable in the history of the world, not because
of any particular distinction in itself, but because it
furnished a striking opportunity to attest to civiliza
tion the usefulness of wireless telegraphy and demon
strate its practical value not only to journalism but to
commerce.

giving to the massing of troops, the shelling of ar
mored trains and the general confusion of war in
South Africa. The British press censor must be a
relative of the gentleman employed in like capacity
at Manila.
THE MISHAP TO THE SHAMROCK.
REGRET at the mishap to the Shamrock will
diminish the pleasure the American people feel
in the second victory of the Columbia. Sir
Thomas Lipton has shown himself to be so genuine
a sportsman and so thoroughly manly in all his ac
tions in connection with the race that he has won
the esteem and friendship of Americans, and in his
iii luck he has the cordial sympathy of all.
Of course the accident was one of the things the
j builders of the Shamrock should have guarded
I against. The object of the international contest is to
determine which nation can build the yachts best
fitted for ocean racing. Strength and stanchness are
qualities which in such yachts must count for as
much as speed. If in their efforts to gain a vast
spread of canvas the British overtax the strength of
the mast or other parts of their yacht they must
bear the loss. That was agreed upon at the time the
terms of the race were settled, and the rule is as fair
; for the one as the other.
That fact, however, will not in any way diminish
the regret over the accident. It is desired the races
should be free from mishaps to cither side. The
occurrence is the more regrettable because the race
in which it took place was one in which the British
have always claimed their yacht would show its best
points. If the Shamrock be beaten in the race on
Thursday she will not have another chance over a tri
angular course. Sir Thomas has not had the "Lipton
luck*' in this venture. He is a game sportsman, how
ever, and is not likely to be daunted by one disaster.
He has repeatedly said that if beaten in this match he
will come back next year with a better yacht. From
| that we may draw the conclusion he will enter the
next race with a stronger topmast, and the Ameri
can people will be glad if Iris good luck return and
give him weather which will show his gallant yacht at
i its best.

PAPA'S DARLING BOY.
DEWEY AND THE PRESIDENCY.
THE newspapers throughout the country have been discussing Admiral Dewey
and the Presidency. A large number consider that it will be the part of
wisdom for the fighting sailor to steer a clear course far away from the
turbulent sea of politics. Others are of the opinion that he has had honors
in abundance, while many of them enthusiastically declare the hero of Manila
Bay to be the ideal man for the place, and that, if nominated by either party,
he would sweep the country.
Brief editorial opinions follow:
SACRAMENTO BEE.
Dewey and Schley occupy positions to-day more exalted than the Presidency.
Either would be foolish to run the risk of marring his record by such mistakes
as were made by U. S. Grant. Besides, it is very doubtful if either Dewey or
Schley would make a good President. ,
Â» â¢ Â»
SAX JOSE HERALD.
The talk about making Dewey the Democratic candidate for President will
probably all end in talk. It is not likely that he would accept the nomination.
Â» Â« Â»
ARIZONA REPUBLICAN.
So far as drawing the hero of Manila into politics is concerned, the admiral
ought to be allowed to rest. He certainly has no intention of permitting the
politicians to juggle with his name.
â¢ â¢ â¢
SACRAMENTO RECORD-UNION.
All the acts and expressions of Dewey since he has come so prominently
into public life warrant the belief that he proposes to leave politics to those who
have been raised to it and have lived all their lives in a political atmosphere.
â¢ * Â» \
LOS GATOS MAIL.
To accept the candidacy for the Presidency next year, no matter of which
party, would be the worst thing that could happen to "our bold sailor boy."
â â¢â¦ , *
CHICAGO JOURNAL.
It is right for the American people to admire Dewey for his soldierly qualities
and reward him for doing his work so well. But if they are a cool-headed and
a shrewd people they will call on statesmen for statesmanship and keep their
warriors for war.
â¢ â¢ â¢
FARGO ARGUS.
If political straws show how the wind blows, and partly revealed signs of the
times indicate anything, then look out for a red-hot Democratic Dewey boom.
â¢ â¢ â¢
PITTSBURG DISPATCH.
Admiral Dewey has shown qualities of tact and judgment which might be
valuable in the White House. But the probabilities rise very nearly to the
stage of certainty that those very qualities will keep him out from between the
upper and nether millstones of politics.
* â¢ â¢ â¢
NEW WHATCOM REVEILLE.
Admiral Dewey would no doubt make a good President, but we venture the
assertion that the hero of Manila has had all the worry he wants and is receiv
ing enough glory and adulation to last him the rest of his days.
â¢ Â» â¢
SAN JOSE MERCURY.
Dewey has nothing to gain and he has all to lose either in the Presidency or
in pursuit of it. It is to be hoped that his great common-sense, combined with
his deep patriotic sense, will give him wisdom to heed his own calm judgment
and to put aside tho temptations of scheming President-makers.

should hesitate seriously before determining to compromise his fame by becom
ing a political candidate.
NASHVILLE AMERICAN.
We cannot be blind to the strength of Dewey nor his eminent fitness for the
exalted office of President. But in our opinion we shall have to content ourselves
with Admiral Dewey. His candidacy for the highest oflice in the gift of the
people is impracticable.

If called to the Presidency he couldn't refuse it, and it nominated by either
party without a platform he would sweep the country like a hurricane.

COLUSA SUN.
Dewey has been talked about as a candidate for the Presidency, but it is to
be hoped that he will not run. He has so far carried a level head, and has re

SAN D I EGAN SUN.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, OCTOBER 18. 1899.
SWINGING INTO LINE
FOR HON. HORACE DAVIS
Republican Committee Organize
for Immediate Work.
Movement in Assembly Districts for Davis
and Good Governmentâ Pheian and
McNab Alarmed.
Â£____jHE new executive committee of the
Republican County Committee, Al
fred Bouvler chairman, met at the
L History buildins last night and
adopted a plan of campaign. Per
manent organization at the â¢ }"â¢
was effected without delay. The chair
man and secretary of the County Com
mittee were chosen as such oflicers of the
executive body. Max Goldberg was elect
ed vice chairman and will preside when
Mr. Bouvler cannot be* on deck.
A proposition presented by Alex Tru
man to create a campaign committee of
seven members elicited discussion. Mr.
McCarthy desired that the duties of the
campaign committee fhould be clearly de
fined at the outset so that no clash should
occur later on.
Mr. Truman understood that it would
he the duty of the campaign committee
to provide * for meetings in the various
Assembly districts, to see that halls wore
engaged, bands hired, speakers secured
and proper announcement be given of the
meetings. In this work committeemen
representing the Assembly districts should
of course lend a helping hand. Mr. Tru
man referred to the necessity of present
ing plain facts to the people. Mention
was made that Mayor Phelan was patting
himself on the breast and claiming the
credit for the reform work accomplished
by Supervisors Dr. Perrault, John Lack-
I mann and Albert I lever.
Mr. McCarthy feared that the campaign
I committee might usurp the powers and
! privileges of the district committeemen,
j ills apprehension of danger in this re
! gard was removed when lt was resolved
that the chairman and secretary of the
executive body should be ex-offlclo mem
bers of the campaign committee, and that
there should be weekly meetings of the
committee throughout the campaign.
Max Goldberg Insisted tliat there should
be a committee clothed with authority to
; act and to be accountable to the general
bod} for the engagement of halls, the
hiring of bands and the disbursement of
money for the expenses of the various
j meetings. Mr. Goldberg advocated a vig
j orous campaign. He was encouraged by
I the report of Republican unity and the
evidences of Republican enthusiasm, but
recognized the necessity for active work
In each of the Assembly districts. He
| contended that certain powers should be
delegated to the campaign committee,
j and that the committeemen in the respec
i tive Assembly districts should co-operate
i with the campaign committee to bring out
'. tho voters to attend the meetings.
Alex Truman's motion that the chair
I appoint a campaign committee of seven
I prevailed. Later in the evening Chair
man Bouvler named the following to com
pose the committee:
Alex. Truman,
J. R. Hillman,
H. C. Henderson,
A. J. Martin,
L. A. Rea,
I. Ascherm,
J. R. McNamara,
The subject of securing quarters sepa
rate from the headquarters of the Horace
Davis Central Club was discussed. A. J.
Martin moved that a committee of three
be appointed to engage suitable rooms,
but withdrew the motion in favor of a
suggestion made by Mr. Brandt that the
chairman be authorized to select the quar
ters. Mr. Bouvler spoko of desirable
rooms in the Baldwin Annex, already fur
nished.
Alter effecting permanent organization
1 the committee went into executive ses
sion to discuss detailed plans for conduct
ing the campaign to a victorious finish..
Reports were submitted showing that
many prominent citizens of Republican
faith who had given their support to
Mayor Phelan in two contests were now
using their energies and Influence to se
cure tlie ''â â â â  '."ii of Horace Davis.
From every district of the city word
comes that the old-line Democrats who
were excluded from a voice in party
councils by Phelan and McNab
have resolved to withhold their
' support from the nominees of the McNab
convention, The Committee of One Hun
dred will be asked to get its votes from
the source where it derived authority to
manage the partyâ the Democratic
State Central Committee.
The Republican leaders are now con
vinced that Horace Davis will be elected.
He will be elected by a large majority of
the Republicans who supported Phelan
last November and who will return to
their party fold. Pheian's success last
-nil. or was obtained by the knifing of
Judge Maguire Phelan Democrats voted
for Gage in return for Republican votes
for Phelan, and so it happened that Pat
ton '.â ! Maguire were slaughtered. Spe
cial precautions will be taken to prevent
such knifing tactics at the next election.
McNab Democrats v.ill vote for Phelan.
the free silver or Bryan Democrats will
vote for the ticket of the People's party
if the candidate for Mayor thereon is not
objectionable, and the Republicans will
vote for Horace Davis and the entire Re
publican ticket.
After the adjournment of the executive
session hist night Alfred Bouvler an
nounced the appointment of A. W. Branch
os assistant secretary of the County Com
mittee. Mr. Branch, who has had expe
rience in campaigns with the Republican
State Central Committee, will enter at
once upon the discharge of his duties.
The Republican nominees have been in
vited to meet the executive committed nt
j the County Committee headquarters 723
Market street, tonight at 8 o'clock", to
! confer with that body on campaign ques
i tions.
DISTRICT DEMOCRATS.
Municipal Nominees Plead in
California Hall for Elec
tion.
The district campaign of the local De
mocracy began last night with a mass
meeting for the Forty-third District Club
ln California Hall. It was long after 8
o'clock before the crowd was sufficiently
large to warrant calling the meeting to
order. The chairman, P. 11. McCarthy,
then called attention to previous local
Democratic victories and drew from them
an inspiration and a hope for another.
With this dubious encouragement Etnli
Pohll, one of the Democratic nominees for
Supervisors, was introduced. He spoke
thoughtfully of the duties of a Supervisor
under the charter and pledged his hearers
in the event of his election to fulfill every
duty honestly, thoroughly and with unre
mitting energy. Equality of rights with
out distinction of class will be his princi
ple of official action, he said, as a Super
visor. Ho was heartily cheered and gave
place to Mayor Phelan.
The Mayor was received with three
cheers and a tiger. He spoke briefly upon
practically the same lines as on Monday
night. His only important digression was
a discussion of what now appears to be to
him a bugbearâ national politics. Ills
Honor indicates very clearly that he has
not only involved himself in Inconsisten
cies, but has seriously offended members
of hLs own party. If they are Democrats
and believe ln national Democratic prin
ciples and Issues, then he ls not, believing
and advocating only local principles. So
his Honor strove last night to show that
he Is a Democrat no less than a "charter
lst."
He Insisted that the local Democracy Is
in close sympathy to the people; the Re
publican party antagonistic to and fearful
of the people. According to the campaign
logic of his Honor the Democracy has
given us the primary law and the charter
and by right of reward and superior
knowledge is entitled to the Initial admin
istration of city affairs under the charter.
In municipal policy he claimed the De
mocracy is the party of progress, pledged
to permanent and liberal public improve
ments that shall not be delayed. The De
mocracy, he insists, has shown Its fairness
and its purpose to inaugurate a local gov
ernment that shall not be partisan, that
shall take forever from partisan politics
the employes of local departments. He
therefore asked the votes of his hearers
and begged them to go to bed early the
night before election and get up early, be
cause the day is not a legal holiday.
Again he was cheered and gave place to
Dennis Geary, who promised all sorts of
things in the event of his election. The
Mayor is still insisting upon a straight
ticket and it must have been some satis
faction to him last night to see that As
sessor Dodge has seen the error of his
ways and ha 3 been reconciled to his own
reproachful designation of "Knownoth-
Ing" in straight voting.
Ho was positive last night that the
straight Democratic must be voted. His
change of heart came in a night. He had
his charts with him again last night and
it begins to look as if hundreds of inno
cent people are going to be inflicted with
the long story or Assessor Dodge's good
ness as related by Assessor Dodge.
During his . address Assessor Dodge
asked his hearers to believe that he is '
being bitterly opposed by tho capitalistic
class, which, he said, he is forcing to pay
taxes hitherto escaped. "As an instance
of this." the speaker continued, "I will
mention the opposition of The Call to me.
I am opposed by that paper because I
have increased the assessable valuation
of the property of its proprietor by
$600,000."
After he had concluded his address
Assessor Dodge asked the representative
of The Call at the meeting to make no
mention of his remark. "I became ex
cited," he declared, "and did not intend
to say what 1 did. I am sorry I spoke so."
Jeremiah Deasy followed the Assessor
and told briefly and plainly that if elected
Sheriff he will be true to that trust which
makes him the guardian of the interests
of the living, which demand equal justice
for rich and poor. Patrick Boland fol
lowed and promised that if elected Public
Administrator he will be true to the more
sacred trust, the trust to the dead, who
have lost the power to defend themselves
and must rely upon the honor of him Into
whose hands their affairs have passed. '
Most of the other Democratic nominees
followed with short addresses. George
Cabaniss, Edmor.d Godchaux, James P.
Booth, J. F. Dwver, A. B. Maguire, P. J.
Curtis, Osgood Putnam and Franklin K.
Lane spoke upon the Issues of the munici
pal campaign, and the assembly adjourned
with three cheers for the ticket.
A REPUBLICAN RALLY.
A large and enthusiastic meeting ot the
Thirty-fourth Assembly District Repub
lican Club was held Monday evening at
Mission Native Sons' Hall on Seventeenth
street. Permanent organization was ef
fected, Clifford MeClellan being elected
president and Edward W. Close secretary.
Eloquent addresses were delivered by
Senator F. W. Burnett. R. B. MeClellan.
George Dietterle and others In support of
the nominees of the Republican party
amid great enthusiasm.
The following resolution was unani
mously adopted:
Be it resoved. That this club do hereDy
indorse the nominees of the Republican
party now before the people for the com
ing municipal election in this city.
The club quartet rendered several plead
ing campaign songs, which met with de
mands for more, and which were grace
fully given. A meeting will be held next
Monday evening at the same hall.
YOUNG REPUBLICANS.
The Young Men's Republican Club was
organized last evening at 7S Shipley street.
After numerous speeches and a good timo
socially the following named officers Were
elected: President, J. lieno; vice presi
dent, George Green; sergeant at arms,
Terrence McCabe; secretary, Horatio
Waterhouse. Fifty citizens signed the
roll and several stirring speeches were
made and S. M. Godsell and Charles Box
ton were cheered to the echo. The presi
dent Informed the members that there
would be an array of speakers at the next
meeting.

CONFIDENT REPUBLICANS.
Five hundred members of the different
Republican clubs of the Thirty-fifth Dis
trict assembled at 3296 Mission street last
evening and organized a joint club for the
purpose of taking an active port in the
coining election. It will be known as the
"Horace Davis Republican Club." W. F.
Warren and E. H. Herrick were elected
president and secretary respectively.
Meetings are to be held every evening in
different parts of the district. Last even
ing ex-Senator T. C. Maher and .Tud.-,e
Scnell were present and addressed the
assemblage.

REPUBLICANS LINED UP
The Thirty-ninth Assembly District
Club has opened headquarters at 407 Hyde
street for the campaign. The head
quarters will not close, day or night, until

banner Republican district of the city and

jority from the Thirty-ninth. With such
stanch workers as Thomas H. McCarthy,
the president of the club; Frank 1).
Worth, A. A. I.otiderback, Amos Currier
and others there will be shoulder-striking
hits for good government in that club.

NEW OPERA WILL BE
PRODUCED NEXT WEEK
"THE CONSPIRATORS" WILL BE
PUT ON BY MOROSCO.
Music by H. J. Stewart and Libretto
by Clay M. â Favorable
Opinions Expressed by
the Critics.
On Monday evening next an entirely
original comic opera, "The Conspirat
ors," will be produced by the Morosco
Opera Company at the Grand Opera
house. The music has been composed by
IT. J. Stewart and the libretto is the
work of the well-known playwright, Clay
M. Greene. It will receive Its first pro
duction on any stage next Monday even
ing.
The scene is laid In Spain during the
middle sixteenth century. The plot Is ro
mantic and at the same time comic.
By those who have an opportunity of
reading the opera and hearing the music
the composition is said to resemble In
style that of Sir Arthur Sullivan, and the
libretto the Gilbertian order. The char
acters are such that will allow clever In
terpretation on the part of the Morosco
Opera Company. One striking feature of
the opera is the Introduction of a number
of pretty and catching songs, running the
gamut from solo to sextet.
Among the principal numbers are in
cluded: Solo, "An Avalanche of Petti
coats"; song and ensemble, "The Inev
itable Thing"; solo, "When I Am Free";
duet, "The Lady Killer"; solo, quartet
and chorus. "Loving's Quite the Thing to
Do"; quartet, "Let l's One and All in
Blissful Thrall"; "The Conspirators'
Chorus"; Bravura song, "El Castlllno a
la Mode"; solo. "Why Is This World
Made Up of Sighing?" song. "1 Do De
vote My Life to You"; song, "What a
Distressing Quandary"; song, "Indiscrim
inate Love"; "The Kiss Duet"; sextet.
"We Saw It With Our Eyes"; duet, "A
Socail Fad"; song, "Why Should Wom
an's Heart Go Begging?" duet, "This Is
No Life but Love"; recitative and ensem
ble, "Come to the Altar"; quarreling duet,
"Chorus of Mad Maidens"; "The Savage
and the Boomerang." Great attention has
been paid to the choruses, which will be
found fascinating and melodious. A
charming incident of act three will be a
novel ballet of beautiful dancing girls,
which has been invented for the occasion
by the stage manager of the Grand Opera
house, Charles 11. Jones. This will be the
cast: Don Juanlto Alverado, Edith Ma
son; Don Umberto Alverado, his tern pa
rent, Arthur Wooley; Friar Santal, his tu
tor, Nace Bonville; Don Castanetto, a
court fopling, Thomas H. Persse; Don
Fillipo Cassanova, chief of the conspira
tors, William Wolff; the King of Spain,
Wlnfred Goff; the Queen of Spain, Bes
sie Fairbairn; Isabella Cassanova, Don
Fillipns daughter, Hattie Belle Ladd;
Ghona Malarin, Georgie Cooper; Mariana,,
Jeanette Fredericks; Serphina, Ida St.
Aubin; Sister Alicia, mother superior of
the convent, Alice Gray; Ferdinand, a
page, Mabel Hilliard; monks, nuns, ladies
and gentlemen of the Spanish court.

PAT READY
IS DEFEATED
BY CREEDON
Put Out in the Eighth Round
After Taking Much Pun
ishment.
Speclal Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK. Oct. 17.â At the Broadway
Athletic Club to-night Dan Creedon of
Australia knocked out Pat Ready of
"Washington in the eighth round of what
was 'â ! have been a twenty-five round
bout at 158 pounds. Ready made a fair
showing, but was no match for the shifty
Australian, and finally succumbed to
Dan's superior eleven
Creedon was fat and Blow on hi? feet,
â¢chile Ready looked to be trained to the
hour. When time was called they came
together several times without landing.
Dan tlnallv sent his right to the body, and
as they clinched Ready sent in two short
right hooka to the jaw. Â,,,,, ,
ZJ>an led off in the second with left hooks
to the head that made Ready stagger,
but Ready came right back with the
right across Dan's jaw. After a clinch
Ready slipped to the floor. Ready rushed
in the third and swung, but Dan was out
of reach. ' 're< don then stepped in and
swung bis right to body and left to the
head. Ready countered with left and
right to Dan's jaw, and it looked for a
moment as If Creedon would be knocked
out lie recovered quickly and with a left
to wind and right to the jaw dropped
Ready to the floor. Â vJ -
Dan walked to his corner as If it was
nil over, but Ready was up at the count
and before Dan could finish him the bell
Creedon tried the body with the right In
the fourth and Ready slugged back with
both hands to the head. Dan then swung
his left to the body and Ready countered
on the jaw. Another right on the jaw
dropped Ready to the floor, but the bell
rang before he could be counted out.
Ready did well in the fifth, countering
Dan's "right to the body with a heavy
swing on the jaw. < "re. 'don continued to
do the forcing, but Ready was shifty and
finished the round strong. Ready con
tinued to Improve In the sixth, giving Dan
blow for blow until Creedon crossed him
with a right and sent him to the floor.
He was up at the count and Dan sent it
over again and Ready fell on his back.
He regained his feet and stayed away
for the rest of the round.
They fought through the seventh and
most "of the eighth with honors even.
Just as the round was about to close Dan
uppercu! his right to the chin and Ready
went down and out.

Murchv Will Remain
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Oct. 17.â
Trainer "Tot" Murphy will remain at
Stanford throughout the entire year as
coach for the varsity baseball and track
men. This announcement was happily
received to-day in athletic circles, as Dr.
Murphy bas already endeared himself to
his men. For four years .Murphy played
shortstop on Yale's team, sides being
captain during his senior year in college.
To the professional world he is quite as
well known, since he played with the New
York club men for two seasons. Besides
being a coach, Mr. Murphy is a gradu
ated physician from Pennsylvania! which
makes ids services as a trainer exceed
ingly valuable.

Purtell Knocks Out Needham.
LEADVIL.L.E, Colo.. Oct. 17.â Two
thousand people saw Paddy Purtell knock
out Danny Needham of St. Paul In one
of .}.. hardest battles ever fought here.
For five rounds the men fought evenly,
Purtel rushing frequently, but was
blocked by his antagonist. in the fifth
round there was a mixup and both men
landed heavily, but Purtell, with a left on
the neck, had his man groggy and
finished him witl) a terrific right swing
on the law. Needham waa unconscious
for fully a minute.
Cycling Record Lowered.
BIRMINGHAM, Ala., Oct. 17.â Thirteen
hundred persons witnessed some fine
bicycle races at the Coliseum track to
night. The world's eight-lap track record
for one mile was lowered ten seconds by
Jay Eaton and B. Stone of Denver on
a motor tandem. The distance was cov
f-red In 1:37 2-5. The record was 1:48, made
by Michael and Johnson on the Memphis
track two years ago.

DANIEL WAS
PLAYED FOR
A "KILLING."
Caesar Young's Two- Year-
Old Put a Crimp in the
Ring at Oakland.
Caesar Toung, the St. Louis bookmaker, !
carved a large-sized, cherry-ripe water
melon at Oakland yesterday with a two
year-old rejoicing in the name of Daniel.
The youngster started in a handicap on
October 5, finishing absolutely last. In
the opening scramble yesterday for maid
ens, with a field of eleven lining up at the
! barrier, he was played down from 30 to 4
and 6 to 1 and won ail the way. His vic
tory can. in a measure be ascribed to the
. start, for Jack Ward beat the gate. Alee,
the favorite, a brown colt, by St. George-
Levee, owned by Garnet Ferguson, was
best, but got away from the post none too
well. He ran a very impressive race, fin
ished third, seven lengths behind Slsquoc.
The ring had quite a crimp put in it over
the two-year-old's win.
In the other events decided favorites In
| the betting finished first on three occa
* sions. The track was still a trifle slow,
! but to-day will find it in as good condition
as before the rain.
Bald-laced Satsuma made his initial bow
'to tne public in the laat race. He looked
I well and warmed up as if fit to crack a
record. The crowd packed him from 2 to
1 to i) to 5 favoritism, and Humidity led
: from start to finish. The mare stopped so
I badly in her last race that she was prac
â  tlcaliv overlooked In the belling, her odds
receding to 7 and 8 to I. The six furlongs
were covered in 1:15 Hat. the best perform
ance of the day. Horton at the start com
manded first call in the books, but went
back and was beaten for the show by

SNOWBOUND HUNTERS.
F. H. Bushnell and "Johnnie" Cole-
man Endure Hardships in the
Sierras.
F. H. Bushnell and Bookmaker
"Johnnie" Coleman arrived home last
night after a siege of hardships in the
mountains back of AVeber Lake, where
they were snowbound for over a week.
Bushnell. Coleman and "Joe" EpÂ»lnger
left here several weeks ago for Weber
Lake, where they beat the thick under
brush for game and whipped the streams
for trout. The trio pitched their camp in
the mountains back of the lake, many
miles from civilization.
Epplnger tired of the sport quickly and
return..', home, leaving his companions to
stay longer.
A day or so later a severe snowstorm
set in and Bushnell and Coleman had to
brave the elements. The snow fell
heavily, and before they realized their
position they were hemmed in. At the
inn at Weber Lake it was known that the
hunters were camping somewhere back
in the mountains, and when the weather
cleared up a searching party was Bent out
to locate them. They searched for several
days and finally located the snowbound
sportsmen in a lonely canyon.
Several times during the snowstorm
Bushnell and Coleman attempted to make
their way out of the mountains, but each
time they had to turn back. i'ntil the
rescuing party reached them they were
beginning to think they would never see
civilization again. Both vow they will
never go into the mountains again with
out 1 a guide.

UNITED STATES
LOSES NOTHING
Alaskan Agreement Is
Being Completed.
Â»
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON", Oct. 17.â Immediately
upon Secretary Hay's return to-day prep
arations began at the State Department
for the completion of th*Â» modus Vivendi
relative to the Alaskan boundary. Gen
eral Foster was hard at work upon the
details of the modus and the expectation
was that ln the course of a day or two
the agreement would be In effect. The
negotiations of late havo been entirely in
the hands of Secretary Hay and air.
Tower, the British Charge here. It has
not been determined even yet whether ,
the agreement defining the boundary
temporarily shall take the form of a docu
ment signed by both parties or be merely
a series of notes, but In either case it will
be Just as effective as a regular modus
Vivendi, binding both parties to observe
the boundary laid down temporarily.
State Department officials are confident
that Americans will have no cause to
complain that their rights have been
abandoned when the full scope of the
agreement is made known, while, on the
other hand, the Canadians cannot claim
properly that they have lost any right
that they have enjoyed.
The purpose of this particular effort at
a modus was to regulate the boundary
line on the west side of the Lynn Canal.
The two parties bad placed the line of
demarcation on Chileat and White
passes right at the top of the passes, and
there has never been the slightest fric
tion at those points. But. because of the
fact that the westernmost of the three
passesâ namely, Chilcat Passâ was fully
forty miles removed from the sea, the
same rule could not be applied by our

The San Mateo Coursing Association
will hold its first draw to-night at Pythian
Castle. The gates of the new park will
be thrown open to the public for the first
time on Saturday. The card for the two
days' meeting will be an open stake, for
which good prizes have been offered. The
winner will receive $250. the runner-up
$150 and others In proportion. C. J. Jerome
will Judge the coursing, while Arthur
Massey will handle the "slips."
The draw for Union Park will be held
in the same building at the same hour.

fore, recourse has been had to another
expedient and the line of demarcation will
run along the Klehana Kiver and from
a point near Klukwan, cross to a moun
tain on the southwest.
The effect will be to give the United
States control of the tide waters, the
British being fifteen miles above; to main
tain the American control of the new and
Important Porcupine country, and, lastly,
to save the rights of all American miners
who are now on the Canadian side of the
line.
It is understood that the modus will
live at the pleasure of both parties to lt.
There will be no date fixed for Its expira

CATTLE ENDANGERED.
Large Herds Imprisoned by Snow in
the Mountains.
' PLACERVILLE, Oct. 17.â The road over
the summit is now open and the large
bands of dairy and stock cattle, number^
ing thousands, that find summer pasture
age ln Lake Valley are now passing
through this city on their way to winter
pasturage in this and Sacramento coun
ties.
Grave fears are entertained that many
cattle that were pastured on the higher
ridges will not be saved. Bayne Bros.,
extensive cattle raisers of Coloma, have a
large band imprisoned ln the snow back
of Pyramid Peak. The snow at that point
Is reported to be nearly six feet ln depth.
Barrett. Orelli, Basi and other cattle
men have large numbers yet In the moun
tains, but hope is felt that if the present
weather continues they will be able to
save them.
Whitecaps Harass Negroes.
BARNESVILLE, Ga., Oct. 17.â An in
vestigation Into the disturbances here, as
a result of which the local military com
pany was called out last night to protect
the Oxford cotton mills from a supposed
mob of negroes, shows that the negroes
came to the town seeking protection from
whitecaps. The negroes claim some ot
their race were roughly handled by the
whitecaps. The Capital City Guards, a
military company of Atlanta, which had
been summoned to its armory to await
orders to come to Barnesville, has been
dismissed.
GENTLEMEN
DRIVERS IN
THE SULKY
Golden Gate Park Driving; As-
sociation Offers an Ettract-
ive Programme
The members of Golden Gate Park j
Driving Association met at the Palace |
Hotel last night and completed arrange
ments for their autumn meeting. This ,
will be held at Ingleside racetrack on j
Saturday.
The most interesting feature of the j
programme will be a double team race. |
in the olden days this was a favorite j
form of sport, but in recent years it* has
languished. It was revived successfully \
on the circuit, which encouraged the ama- I
teur drivers to try their hand at the |
exciting sport. With three or four teams â 
to start, the event should be an interest- i
ing one. It will be best two out of three I
heats, as will all the other races. The
remaining ones are a 2:20 class, 2:40 class
and the second contest for the Frank G.
O'Kane trophy.
After extended discussion, it was de
cided to do away with the race which it j
was intended should be trotted under un- |
usual conditions. It will be straight two
in three heats, as it was believed that |
otherwise an Injustice would be done :
some owners. The object of the associa- ;
tion is to provide good sport for its mem- i
bers and to increase the number of gen- !
tlemen drivers. The purse in each race i
will be $100, divided into three moneys of |
60 per cent, 25 and 15. In the cup race J. j
C. Ohlandt has one victory to his credit,
but must win it three times in all to make I
it liis property. Admission will be free to
the races. 'The following officers will
handle the meting: nB_VS
T. J. Crowley, starter; R. F. Gallagher,
M. M. Donnelly and L. Richardson,
judges; J. G. Chesley, F. L. Clauson, F. |
Vetter and G. Lapham, timers; Dr. Ira i
B. Dalzell, marshal and assistant starter.
UNITE TO FIGHT
THE RAILROAD
Meeting of Citrus Fruit
Growers and Packers.
â¦
Special Dispatch to The Call.
RIVERSIDE, Oct. 17â Southern
California Fruit Exchange and the out
side packers have united to fight rail
road discriminations in transportation.
The Citrus Fruits Growers', Packers'
j and Shippers' Association in session here
1 this afternoon adopted, with but one dis
senting vote, a resolution to carry the
fight into the Federal courts If necessary,
and the Southern California Fruit Ex
change will co-operate. The only dis
senting vote was the Earl Fruit Com-
Eany, which stands for and reaps the
benefit of a private car line. The reso
lution is as follows:
Whereas, The welfare of any Industry de
mands that freight rate discriminations do not
exist in any form, and as shipping factors en
gaged In handling the product of the citrus
orchards of California we view with especial
favor any plan promulgated by the carriers
that promises relief from the pernicious and ille
gal system of rebates from the taTiff rates, and
will lend our -Tdlal assistance as Individuals
and an organization to any reasonable plan
for the absolute elimination of freight rate dis
criminations. We believe that the payment of
rebates perverts the influence of shipping fac
tors from the interests of the growers to that of
the carrying lines. We protest against and will
antagonize any plan of the railroad companies
| which effects only a partial elimination of the
rebating and which grants special privileges
i to favored Interests through the medium of pri-
I vate car lines; and.
Whereas, The present transportation arrange
' ments, as provided for the citrus traffic of 1899
--1900, under the pretext of accomplishing the
elimination of freight concessions, do clearly
provide >r the special privileges of car lines,
which privileges have been granted to some
shippers and denied to others, and as this In
side advantage, enjoyed by certain Interests,
â¢ can only result ln disadvantage to the Industry
and the community It supports; therefore be It
Resolved. That the policy of the Initial lines,
as now understood, is openly defiant of the In
terstate commerce laws and inimical to the
welfare of the Industry, for which reason the
powers of the Federal courts should be In
voked to correct the flagrant evils now exist
ing.
The Jamaica treaty was considered and
a resolution adopted to co-operate with
the Southern California Citrus Traffic,
Committee to raise a fund to defeat the'
confirmation of the treaty. This resolu
tion was sent, with greetings, to the
growers' meeting in session here at the
same time. The grawers' meeting resolv
ed unanimously to levy one-fifth of a
â¢cent per box for the tariff fund.
TROUBLES OF MRS.
ANNA KLINE RIKERT
President of the Stockton and Tuol
umne Company Involved in
Litigation.
STOCKTON, Oct. 17.â John MeCormick
yesterday recovered Judgment In Judge
Budd's court against the National Se
curity Company for approximately $7000.
The suit was an outgrowth of the litiga
tion over the "Woman's Road," of which
Mrs. Rikert was the president, and which
was incorporated under the name of the
Stockton and Tuolumne Railway Com
pany. MeCormick furnished meat for the
workmen and advanced money from time
to time to the president of the road.
Being convinced that it was a bad debt
to have outstanding he began suit to re
cover and got an attachment on a lot
of rails which the company had stored
in this city. Mrs. Rikert was very anx
ious to get the attachment released and
sought the aid of the security company.
For the usual fees and the custody of the
rails the company agreed to release the
attachment. The rails would revert to
the custody of the Sheriff ln case a judg
ment was secured by the plaintiff.
In due time this judgment was secured
and demand was made on the security
company for the return of the rails. The
company could not return them, as they
had been sold. Suit accordingly followed
on the bond. The company may not lose
entirely on the deal, as it Is presumed
that it secured itself out of proceeds of
| the sale of the rails. Notice of appeal was
| given.
Want a New Charter.
PASADENA, Oct. 17.â T0-day President
Daggett of the Board of Trade memorial
ized the City Council for a new charter,
asking that a committee of three be ap
pointed to look up and draft a new char
ter. He asked the Council to call an elec
tion of the people to elect fifteen free
holders, they to call another election on
the charter. The present charter is sixth
class. A special charter Is needed, be
cause now sufficient sewers, parks and
other Improvements cannot be had. nor
can kindergartens be introduced In the
public schools on account of the. fact that
the District Attorney, who Is not In fa
vor of them, is given too much power.
Tho Council filed the memorial and ex
pressed a favorable sentiment, but made
the objection that sewers and other Im
provements demanded by the -Board of
Trade would cost between half a million
and a million dollars. Last Saturday the
ladies of the Shakesepeare Club, the prin
cipal literary organization of this city,
met and bound themselves to work for
the new charter.
Captain O'Brien Dead.
SAN JOSE, Oct. 17.â Captain Charles
F. O'Brien, for years connected In an
official way with the mines at Almaden,
died there last night. ,He had been ill
for a long time. He was a native of
Michigan, but came to the mines in 1865
and has been there since. He was 65
years old.
OLYMPIC MEN
RENDEZVOUS
AT ESCALLES
A Notable Gathering of Ath
letes Arranged by the '
Club's Directors.
The members of the Olympic Ath
letic Club will "get together" next
Sunday in greater numbers than
they have done in many years.
They are to be the guests of the club at
Escalles, a resort between Sausalito and \
San Rafael, where, in addition to a fine j
dinner, they will be entertained by noted I
funmakers, besides taking part in im- j
promptu games.
In the early history of the club any af
fair that attracted one member attracted j
all. Whenever Haley, Schifferstein, J
Flynn, O'Kane or any of the club's sprint- j
ers were competing they always had a
following. In later years the big mem- I
bership of the club has been scattered
among the "annexes." The wheelmen i
flocked by themselves. The gunmen, |
swimmers, runners, athletes, handball- :
players and others all had seperate or- I
ganizations within the parent body. Each
developed and thrived, but sapped in a â 
degree the life of the big club. It is to
bring all the members together in one i
great body that the affair of Sunday has j
been arranged.
The officers of the various annexes will
endeavor to bring out their full strength.
The affair was originally suggested by
Captain George James. Leader John A.
Hammersmith, John Elliott and other di- |
rectors approved of it and are working iO
make It a success. James W. Coffroth, j
"Tom" Cunningham and others who were
responsible for the success of former out
ings of the Wheelmen, on the same lines, j
are interested in the successful outcome
of this venture.
The members of the club can go to the
rendezvous on either the 8, 10 or 11:30 a. m.
boat by way of Sausalito. The Wheelmen
may go on one of the early boats and
reach Escalles after a short spin over the
; road, possibly from San Rafael.
BOOM IN CHINESE GAMBLING.
Four New Fantan Houses Opened
for Business.
The Chinese quarter of tho city is
showing unmistakable signs of being af
fected with the tidal wave of prosperity
which is sweeping over the country. The
prosperity of Chinatown, however, arises
from a purely local causeâ the return of
cannery hands from up the coast.
The first evidence of an influx of "easy
money" is seen in the gambling dens
which abound for the delectation of Mon
golians, and during the past few weeks
the business of these shady houses has
been so brisk and they have been run
with so little police molestation that new
places have blossomed out, some of them
most elaborate affairs, and now the Ce
lestial sport finds no trouble in enjoying
the pleasures of peegow and fantan.
At 44 Waverly place more than $1000
has been expended in ornate decorations
of a room where all the Chinese gam
are played. This place, as well as one
at 802 Dupont street and one at SID 1 -
Washington street and one on St. Louis
alley. opened Its doors to the Chinese
public during the past week, and reports
from those who have the entree say that
fantan has never before seen such a
boom.
The returning canners have saved up
quite a respectable sum. and the boom in
Mongolian gambling will last until it has
changed hands.

S. A. Bishop, the motorman of the car
that ran over and killed Patrick McGuire

manslaughter, had his case dismissed by

that McGuire's death was the result of an
accident.

ing at 531 Montgomery avenue, swore to a
complaint in Judge Treadwell's court yes
terday for the arrest of her father. Pla
cator F. Valiant, a magnetic healer, on
the charge of failing to provide .for her
sister, Viola, 11 years of age. although his
wife deeded some property to him in Los
Angeles for that purpose.
Frank Thompson, who stole a basket of
clothes from Mrs. L. J. Werner. 1121 Pow
ell street, and when arrested had several
skeleton keys In his pockets, was yester
day sent to the County Jail by Judge
Treadwell for six months on each of two

lars' tools in his possession.

and Bryant streets, was yesterday held to

Judge Treadwell on a charge of assault to
murder, In $2000 bonds.

DEBTS HASTEN
THE DEATH OF
THE ACME CLUB
It Will Cease to Exist After
To-Day â Members Join
Other Clubs.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Oct. 17.
Tlie Acme Club will go out of existence
to-morrow. A constable will sell the per
sonal property of the club to satisfy the
! judgment in favor of the landlords, and
j the athletic organization which began
fourteen years ago in a barn in a back
! yard, with an old pair of boxing gloves
j and with DeWitt Van Court for in
| structor, will pass into history.
To-day President Fitzgerald of the Re
liance Club received a little note from the
Sheriff apprising him of the sale and sug
gesting that as the athletic furnishings
are not likely to cause much competition,
the Reliance Club might possibly become
a customer.
"For several weeks," said .President
Fitzgerald, "the boards of directors of
both clubs have been discussing the ad
visability of uniting. It has long been
apparent that two big athletic clubs can
not prosper in this city. When the at
tachment was levied on the Acme Club
a crisis was reached and as things are
at present about two hundred of the best
athletes of the Acme Club will come to
us. There will be no need of any con
solidation, and it is better that there be
none. No matter which club went to the
other, the remaining club would have to
assume the indebtedness of the other.
There will be no advantage in a formal
union, because each club has a thorough
gymnastic outfit and is well furnished,
and if we bought the Acme outfit we
could only store it to replace our own
in years to come, which would not bo
good business policy.
: "There are now about four hundred
members of the Reliance Club and since
we went into our present fine building
more than one-half of our total indebted
ness has been paid off. With the big ad
dition we shall receive when the Acme
Club goes out of existence I anticipate
no trouble in maintaining a splendid
organization that will win many honors.
In several contests, especially in wheel
contests, the Acme and Reliance clubs
have each nearly scored a victory. With
the pick of the two clubs we could have
done as we pleased, and that is what I
anticipate we shall soon be doing.
"I presume a certain number of the
Acme Club will Join the new West Oak
land Athletic Club, but I cannot see that
this will in any way Interfere with the
progress of the enlarged Reliance Club.
"With only one big club in town giv
ing boxing exhibitions we shall be able
to do better financially in more ways than
one. Our dues at present are $2 50 and
we give about once a month first class
boxing contests, charging $1 for admis
sion. Many men in town who joined tha
club for the express purpose of seeing
these exhibitions quit their membership,
preferring to pay a dollar for each en
tertainment. In future it is very prob
able that we may limit these contests
entirely to club members."
The Acme Club has run in arrears on
its lease $2400. Abrahamson Brothers,
the lessors, after putting on an attach
ment, reduced the amount of the arrears
to $1600 on condition that this sum should
be paid at once, and on the same condi
tion they also reduced the rent from $175
to $125 per month. The Acme members,
however, who have carried along their
club so many years in the face of great
difficulties, decided that It would be use
less to prolong their agony, and that tha
most practical and feasible thing to do
would be to join the Reliance Club.
A committee from each of the clubs
was appointed and met this evening, in-
President Fitzgerald's office. The Acme
members who intend to join the Reliance
requested that one or two of their in
structors who had been with the club
many years should be taken care of by
the Reliance. Some of these instructors
could have secured employment at the
Reliance Club any time they wished, so
this request was adjusted without diffi
culty. President Fitzgerald admitted that
the records of the Acme Club, from a
purely athletic standpoint, were some
what superior to those of the Reliance,
and said that with a couple of hundred
of the best members of the Acme added
to the Reliance it would guarantee athletic
and financial success in its management
and would relieve the club from the con
tinual efforts that are necessary to main
tain its present high standing.
lt is expected that many of the Acme
members will go to West Oakland. Many
of these Acme boys live in that end of
town, and as the new club has just in
corporated it will doubtless receive quite
a boom by the going of the Acme.
At the meeting to-night of the Acme
and Reliance committees it was decided
that the Reliance should engage Boxing
Instructor Kitchen and Wrestling In
structor Paul Carroll of the Acme.
Obnoxious Loading Law.
PASADENA, Oct. President J. H.
Woodworth of the Pasadena Orange-
Growers' Association to-day received a.re
ply from Manager Edward Chambers of
the Santa Fe freight department at San
Francisco to the complaint of the growers
against the loading edict taking effect No
vember 15. The minimum carload will be
26,000 pounds. The cars used will be 40 and
36 foot cars. This maKes a hardship for
buyers and shippers because oftentimes
buyers want small lots that cannot now
be furnished. They must take large lots
or nothing. Woodworth says he will have
to grin and bear it.
Fire at Elrio.
VENTURA, Oct. 17â Fire destroyed the
general merchandise store of Galvin &
Shorten at El Rio last night. Charles
Grubb always slept in the. front end of the
store and he narrowly escaped death. The
front doors were broken down and the
clerk dragged out of bed. The loss is
$4500. Insurance $2700. The origin of the
fire is unknown.THE CALLS
