I. NEW WORDS AND HOW TO USB
THEM.
Keep a, common writing pad on your
desk. When you hear a new word which
interests you or when you come aoross
one in your reading jot it down. Take
time to look up Its meaning. Frame sen
tences In which this word is used cor
rectly. Incorporate it into your own vo
cabulary by using it in your conversation
â¦lie very first chance you get, not in un
affected way, but as part of your every
day '"*.â¢;. You must learn to distinguish
between words suitable for conversation
and words which are better adapted to
the printed page. Conversational speech
has soul and feeling. Written speech
may be technical, scientific, exact; the
meaning being conveyed wholly by the
words, while In conversation tho meaning
Is conveyed in part by the gesture, the
voice and the personality of the speaker.
Take the following twelve words for your
first lesson and add a dozen more of your
own choosing during the week:
Resumeâ This is a French word which
has come into common use. It is pro
nounced ray-zsu-may, with accent upon
first and last syllables. It means a sum
mary, a- summing up, a condensed ac
count. We may say that a particular
magazine or paper gives a good resume of
current events.
Connoisseur â Pronounced con-1-seur,
with the accent upon the last syllable. A
connoisseur is a critical judge, or rather a
person who is competent to pass critical
judgment upon anything. The word is
most commonly used in speaking of a per
son thoroughly competent to pass judg
ment upou the worK of Borne particular
department of art. A man who is mak
ing a specialty of collecting rare water
colors aud who Is considered from long
experience competent to pass upon a pic
ture offered is said to be a connnoisseur In
water colors.
Altruisticâ This word is now very com- !
monly used. The noun is altruism. It is
opposed to selfishness. Any movement
having for its :hiel end the good of others
may be termed altruistic. Note this word i
In your newspaper and magazine reading.
You are sure to meet it.
.Ghettoâ The name Ghetto is given to the
ifert of a city or town formerly set apart
tor the Jews, aa In Rome or Frankfort- '
on-the- Rhine. At present this name is a
synonym for "Jewish quarter."
Radicalâ Measures are radical when they
are extreme or directly opposed to conven
tional or commonplace methods. A man
is radical when he is the opposite of con- :
servative ln his ideas and methods In po- j
lltical, religious and social affairs. Ihe .
radical breaks away from ail traditions i
and established customs.
Salonâ Pronounced sab-long, with the j
second syllable accented. An apartment ,
in which a company Is to receive; hence,
a fashionable reception or gathering. The :
name salon is specifically applied to peri
odic social reunions in Paris. The name is
also applied to the annual exhibition ln
Paris of works of living artists.
Bohemianâ A person, especially an ar- ,
tlst or literary' man, who leads a free '
life, having little regard to what society |
he frequents and despising the Â»nven
tional. One who prefers adventure and i
speculation to settled industry and who
does not work well ht the harness of or- j
dinary life. This word is pretty -rally !
used in America as the opposite of con
ventional. The bohemian's dress and hab
its una dinners and pleasures are not al- i

Lengths cr distances are measured by
the foot, yard or mile. Land is measured
by the chain, which consists of 100 links
and Is 22 yards long. Areas are measured
by the square foot, square yard or square
mile. Solids are measured by the cubic
inch, cubic foot or cubic yard.
A root of lumber is a solid containing

POSTUM CEREAL.
THE MINISTER'S COFFEE.
Hurt Him Worse Than the Devil Did.
"I used the old-fashioned coffee from
my babyhood, and like many others
thought I could not live without it.
Was In the army four years during the
Rebellion and came out very much
broken In health. I was compelled to
quit the active work of the ministry
for more than a year at a time, suf-
fering terribly from stomach and In-
testinal troubles and ton liver, with
the consequent nervous headache and
prostration, lumbago and rheumatism.
"Four years ago. being confined to
bed with these troubles and ulceration
of the liver, my physician positively
forbade the use of coffee and brought
me some Postum Cereal Food Coffee.
Since that time myself and family have
used no ther coffee. All my Intestinal
trouble has been greatly relieved, and
I no longer suffer from rheumatism
and very little from torpid liver or bil-
iousness.
"I am fully convinced that coffee is
very largely responsible for the greater
part of all stomach, intestinal and livei-
troubles from which people suffer. I
am now 58 years old, and my health
has been so much improved that I do
aa much work as I ever did, and with
about as much comfort. I can con-
scientiously 'attribute it chiefly to the
use of Postum Cereal Food Coffee.
There are several friends who have had
valuable experience with the leaving
off of coffee and the use of Postum
Food Coffee. It gives me pleasure to
add mv testitmony to help humanity
to understand that the very general
cause of sickness Is, 1 consider, largely
attributable to mistakes In the use of
food and drink." Rev. J. A. Flickinger.
pastor of the Lutheran church, Kays
Hill. Pa. Grocers sell Postum Coffee,
and Postum Cereal Co.. Ltd., make it
at Rattle Creek, Mich. '

ways in harmony with the rules or cus
toms of society.
Personalityâ A man's personality is that
which pertains to his person, the things
which characterise him, the impression,
made by ids person. Personality differs
from address. A man of good address ls
a man who is well dressed and of courte
ous, free and gentlemanly speech. A man
of pleasing personality ls a man the good
ness of whose character impresses one im
mediately. A man of strong personality
is a man with strong and effective power
of will.
Bourgeoisâ Pronounced boor-zhwah. This
Word is commonly used in the sense
of unrefined or uncultured. In France the
name bourgeoisie, pronounced boor
ehwah-rt, is used to designate the middle
or artisan class, as distinguished from the
aristocracy.
Stiltedâ Elevated and stiff as If on stilts;
hence the word is used in the sense of me
chanical or unnatural; as, a stilted walk;
a stilted letter; a stilted style; a stilted
speech.
Diplomacyâ The art and practice of con
ducting negotiations between nations. The
name is applied generally in the sense of
tact, or of dexterity and skill in the man
agement of men. We say that a man is
diplomatic when he exercises keen Judg
ment and a cautious policy in securing the
co-operation of other men. The word pol
itic is very similar In meaning, but refers
more to careful personal relations.
Renaissanceâ Pronounced ren-nay-sahns.
This word means a new birth, a revival or
resurrection, and is applied specially to
the period of awakening in arts and let
ters during the last centuries of the dark
ages. The renaissance began in Italy in
the fourteenth century and spread gradu
ally over Western Europe. The name is
applied also to the art and architecture
which belonged to that period of history,
as: "The renaissance style of building
and decoration succeeded the Gothic and
sought to reproduce the forms of classical
ornamentation."
This series will be continued next week.
Let us turn now to a few suggestions and
cautions which will aid us in our written
speech.
When we speak of pure air, pure water
or pure gold we mean air, gold or water
that has been cleared of all extraneous
matter. Similarly, when we speak if pure
language we mean language that has been
cleared of all extraneous words and
phrases. The dross of language consists
of vulgarisms, words and phrases that
should be avoided even in conversation
such as "kid" for child, "kick the buck
et," "you bet," etc.; colloquialisms, words
and phrases admissible in conversation,
but not suitable for written composition,
such as "jiffy" for instant, "sat upon" for
censured, etc. Colloquialisms when used
at the proper time and place may give
force and zest to conversation. A collo
quialism which may be quite proper in the
speech of one person may be wholly out of
place in the speech of another: provincial
isms, words confined to some particular
province or locality; obsolete words; for
eign words which have not been incorpor
ated into our language; newly coined
words. We must not rid our speech of all
its spice and ginger and snap, but it must
be remembered that these qualities cannot
be introduced by words merely. Language
is simply the wire which carries the mes-
sage.
Propriety of language means the using
of words (1) in their proper senses, (2) in
proper connections. Any sense or connec
tion of words may be considered proper
that has the concurrent authority of stan
dard writers who have lived near enough
to our own time to be regarded as safe
guides for present usage. We shall take
up for our lesson next week a few com
mon Improprieties of speech.

MINISTERS AT DINNER.
A dinner wns held at the California
Hotel last evening, the occasion being the
regular monthly meeting of the Congre
gational Club of San Franclsco. Rev. R.
H. Chamberlain presided, and the thirty
gentlemen present listened with pleasure
and gratification to the address of wel
come which he delivered.
The topic discussed after the dinner
had been disposed of was the recent In
ternational convention of Congregational
churches held in Boston, to attend which
went three members of the local club.
: The speakers who held forth ' on the
subject last night were Rev. George C.
Adams, Rev. C. R. Brown. Rev. H. H.
Wycoff and Rev. Walter Frear. Each
one of these gentlemen had something
Interesting and Instructive to say and
which held their audience, until nearly
midnight, when the meeting broke up.
Injured by a Car.
Thomas Brady, 2228 Geary street, at
tempted to board a car at Larkin and
Hayes streets yesterday afternoon and
fell. The platform of the car crushed his
right leg. breaking both bones and dis
locating his hip joint. He was taken to
the Receiving Hospital.
WRECKS ON THE
BEACH AT CAPE
NOME NUMEROUS
Steamers and Schoon
ers Driven Ashore.
â¦ -
EL PRIMERO A LUCKY VESSEL
â¦
BOUGHT FOR A SONG, SHE IS
NOW COINING MONEY.
â¦
Cruiser Newark Takes Mail to Philip
âCity of Papeete Arrives
Prom Tahiti Transport
From Honolulu.
Vessels going to Cape Nome with pas
sengers and general merchandise have a
hard time of it. Many of the smaller
craft drag their anchors and go ashore,
while nearly a third of the merchandise
is lost by the capsizing of the lighters.
Some of the boats that go ashore get
off, but quite a few have left their bones
to bleach on "the golden shore."
E. "Manny" Fogel has Just returned
from a trip to Kotzebue Sound, Golovin
Bay and Cape Nome. Yesterday he was
telling his friends on the front what he
thought of the different districts.
"Kotzebue is a good place to stay away
from," he said. "Golovin Bay is all right,
but it is not a poor man's diggings.
However, at Cape Nome there is gold for
everybody. When I left there the min
ers were washing from $20 to $50 a day
out of the sand of the beach.
"It is a terrible place to land at, how
ever. There is a gravel beach and no
holding ground. In consequence the ves
sels have to stand on and off and land
their passengers and cargo in lighters.
Sometimes the steamer is driven off and
then it occasionally takes her a week to
get back again. Barges going ashore
with provisions are caught in the break
ers and rolled over, and that is the end
of the merchandise. I think I am safe
in saying that a third of the goods that
are sent to Cape Nome are lost by the
' capsizing of lighters.
"The old pilot-boat Caleb Curtis Is a
total wreck. She dragged her anchor,
went ashore among the gold hunters and
was lost. The schooners George Swan,
; R. Eckert and Lady George laid their
bones on 'the golden shore,' while
j the steamer Elk went ashore but
i got off. The steamer Joe Matthews
' was not so successful. She went
! a.shore and nothing but her machin
j cry was saved. The EI Primoro followed
j the Joe Matthews on the beach and
\ her owners thought so little of the chances
: of getting her off that they sold her as
j she lay for $100 to Jim Kennedy, who was
first mate of the steamer Bertha not long
i ago. Kennedy gave George Brown a half
' interest ln the steamer to get her off.
and before 1 left Nome they started for
'. the Siberian coast after a load of rein-
I deer. Kennedy and Brown will get rich
j out of that Investment of $100." Fogel
I brought down quite a sack of gold with

! The cruiser Newark came down from

, sail for Manila via Honolulu and Guam,
early this morning. The postoffice offi
i cials put about ten tons of mail matter
aboard her. Nearly all of It is for the
. Philippines and Guam. Twenty tons of
fresh provisions were also taken aboard
the cruiser, so the crew will not suffer for
want of delicacies during the run to Ma
nila.

The barkentlne City of Papeete arrived

Reinsurance on the American ship

yesterday. She Is now out IS3 days from
Norfolk, Va., for Honolulu, with a cargo
of coal. The general impression among
â¢ shipping men is that she has gone to Ha
â  waii via the Cape of Good Hope. The
British ship Queen Victoria, on which 15
per cent reinsurance was paid, arrived at
Port Townsend yesterday and it now
transpires that the St. Enoch, which is
overdue here from Panama, and on which
15 per cent reinsurance has been paid,
was spoken when fifty-three days out, in
Panama Bay. She was In the doldrums
and was making no headway. The St.
Enoch, therefore, cannot be considered
overdue.
The steamer North Fork has been laid
up to have her boilers repaired and the
steamer Luella will go out on her run
next Saturday. The North Fork will not
be ready for sea again for over a week.

SAILOR KITS MOT TO
BE HELD FOR DEBT
BOARDING-HOUSE KEEPER TO
BE MULCTED.
William Hermann Will Be Obliged
to Pay One Hundred Dollars for
Violating the Maguire Act.
Up to the decision of United States Dis- j
trict Judge Hanford of the State of !
Washington about two months ago, sail- j
ors whose kits had been retained by i
boarding-house keepers or others because i
of debt or for any other reason, had but. 1
little difficulty in recovering their prop
erty under the provision of the Maguire
act. The procedure in such cases was |
i the filing of a criminal complaint and the '
I arrest of the boarding-house keeper.
i When brought before the United States :
! Court Commissioner the defendants in al
! most every case settled the matter by
; surrendering the. clothing or other per
sonal property that had been withheld.
Judge Hanford's decision was to the
effect that criminal proceedings could not
be taken legally under the Maguire act, ,
I and that the proper procedure was tc i
bring, in the name of the United States,
a civil suit in the United States -District
i Court against the boarding-house keeper i
1 or other defendant for the collection of j
the $100 penalty provided for the act. â  ' j
Assistant United States District At- ]
i torney Banning was applied to a few!
> days ago by Francis Manger, a sailor,
'. who complained that William H. Her- '
: mann, the keeper of a sailor boarding- !
house on Steuart street, had retained tils i
sea-going clothing under the plea that 1
â  Mauger owed him for board and lodging, ;
and refused to give up the property.
Mr. Banning notified Hermann that he ]
i was acting in violation or law. and ad
vised him to return. the clothing to Mau
ger, but Hermann refused to .follow the
advice. Mr. Banning thereupon filed a
complaint* in civil process against Her
, mann to recover $100 penalty and costs. I
Half of the penalty goes to the Informer,
| who, in this case, is the sailor. The other j
half and the costs go to the Government. '<
I United States District Attorney Coombs !
will press the suit vigorously for the pro-
I tectlon of sailors, for whose benefit the
Maguire act was passed by Congress.

The largest entry of raw silk on record
at this port was made In the Custom
house yesterday, the Importation being
2320 bales from China and Japan for differ
ent manufacturing centers in the United
States. The importation is valued at
$1,175,296. â -â â¢'--.

I When Mrs. Willard B. Harrington,
! president of the State Red Cross Society.
I called its second annual meeting to order
j in the rooms of the Philomath Club, cor
ner of Bush street and Van Ness avenue,
] yesterday morning, she addressed the as
! sembled delegates and warmly welcomed
i them In the name of the good cause they
j represented. She emphasized, as did
i every report read during the session, that
I the auxiliary should hold together, even
j though it held but one meeting a year,
| as Its members never knew what emer
i gency would call for active service.
I Among the delegates present were the
i following representatives of the various
! auxiliaries: Redwood City, Mrs. S. T.
i Fox; St. Helena, Mrs. J. M. Holihen;
i Oakland, Mrs. Ada Van Pelt, Mrs. John
Bakewell and airs. J. G. Lemmon; San
Jose, Mrs. Addie Frink; Castroville, Mrs.
Grace M. Huxley; Sausalito, Mrs. L. M.
| Harrison; New Almaden, Mrs. F. S. Lo-
I well; Calistoga. Mrs. Cora Towle; Ala
| meda, Mrs. M. F.'McGurn; San Francisco,
: Miss Annie M. Beaver; Oroville, Kate H.
Boynton; Auburn, Mrs. Emma Prewetf.
Visalia, Miss Francis Butz; Vacaville,
Clara H. Heriot; Sacramento, Mrs. Albert
Elkus, and Hanford, Mrs. B. A. Fassett.
Mrs. L. L. Dunbar, secretary of the so
j ciety, read her annual report. She told
of the closing of the Convalescent Home
1 and how it has since been used as a read
! ing room, nurses" home and stopping !
| place for officers and their families, en
| route to the Philippines. She stated that
; 139 patients had been treated at the hos
i pital; that in August last the Red Cross
I Society offered to reopen the home, but
| the Government officials thankfully de
clined the offer, stating that the Govern
| ment was now able to handle the sick and
j Wounded without outside assistance.
C. M. Waage, one of the nurses who
went to the Philippines with the First
California Regiment, detailed at length
his experiences In that distant country.
He also eulogized the splendid work per
formed by Francis A. Blake Jr., who
helped nurse the soldiers back to health
and strength.' Mrs. Greenleaf, the wife
of Colonel Greenleaf, head of the medical
department, followed. She made an ap
peal for literature for the library in
augurated in Manila by the late Colonel
Milev, which she proposes to keep up.

interest to the members of the great or
ganization, was then read by Mrs. Dun
bar: fyTyyy

FOR THE RED CROSS.
Band of the First California Gives
the Second of Their Concerts.
A small but appreciative audience lis
tened with pleasure to the concert" of
the band of the First California Regi
ment at the Alhambra last night. -
This was the second of the series of con
certs which will be given by the band
during the week.
The band selections were Interspersed
with solos, vocal and Instrumental. Mrs.
Eva Tenny, with her songs, was enthu
siastically encored. At the conclusion
of her second selection she was presented
with two splendid bouquets.
To-morrow night the house will be filled
with the boys of the Washington Regi
ment, and in the afternoon the manage
ment will entertain the orphans of San
Francisco. ,
The stage setting of the Filipino. house
affords a realistic background for the
band, the members of which were clad In
their campaign uniforms.

SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver lower. Exchange unaltered.
Wheat relapsed into dullness. Barley quiet. .
Wheat freights firm at better rates.
Oats firmly held, but dull. Rye firm and Corn quiet.
Middlings marked up 50c. No change in Bran.
Hay in better shape than for several months.
Beans continue to advance. Seeds as before quoted.
Potatoes firm and Onions steady.
Butter and Eggs continue to advance. Cheese firm.
Poultry still weak under targe stocks. Game quieter.
Crape's show the effect of the recent rain.
Pried fruits, Honey and Nuts firm and active.
Fair demand for Provisions at unchanged prices.
Plops quoted weaker and dull.
Wool quiet and unchanged. Hides and Leather active and firm.
Beef steady. Mutton firm, other Meats as before.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure has fallen rapidly during the
past twenty-four hours on the Pacific Coast.
There are some Indications of the approach of
a low north of Tillamook Head. The tempera
ture has risen slowly over Central California,
and dry, moderately warm weather has pre
vailed over the fruit drying section. Rain has
fallen from Cape Mendocino northward on the
coast, and conditions are such that showers
may occur over Northern California,
Forecast made at s.i:, Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, October 18, 1899:
For Northern Californiaâ Cloudy Wednesday,
with occasional light showers ;:l extreme north
ern portion: southeasterly winds.
For Southern Californiaâ Fair Wednesday,
for Nevadaâ Cloudy Wednesday.
For Utahâ Cloudy Wedm .-lav.
For Arizonaâ Fair Wednesday; warmer at
Phoenix and east.
For San Frani and- vicinity Cloudy and
threatening In the morning; fair Wednesday;
fresh southerly, changing to westerly, winds.
ALEXANDER G. McADIE,
Forecast Official.
EASTERN MARKETS
New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK, Oct. 17.â There was a short
lived demonstration by the bulls in the Stock
Market to-day, but before trading was sus
pended the early gains were largely cur
tailed and in some cases more than wiped out.
The strength was attributed to various
causes. The reappearance this week upon the
floor of the exchange of several operators who
took a large part in last spring's big bull
market, and who have been away on long
summer vacations, gave something of a fillip
to bullish sentiment. The quiet tone of the
money market yesterday In the face of oper
ations Incident to the change of old Central
racific becurities for new encouraged some
sentiment for a rise. There were one or two
stocks in the list which were notably strong
from Individual causes which were rumored
but not authoritatively disclosed, and which
helped to sustain prices. Among these were
Pullman arid Great Northern .-:-â¢'- i ed, al
though the latter more than lost the early
gains. The Baltimore and Ohio stocks were
conspicuously Strong, the common rising an
extreme 2% and the preferred -. Laclede Gas
continued Its advance and readied as high as
80. The Grangers, Louisville .Southern Pa
cific were quite well taken early in the day,
apparently by public interests. The public was
not In evidence at any time during tha day.
Call muney continued at 6 per cent or below
In spite of the fact that many banks report
their cash still below the legal reserve. But
while the money rate did not tighten, a num
ber of industrial stocks, especially those
classed as "Green" in Wall-street parlance,
showed signs of weakness as might happen if
they were subjugated to discrimination by mon
ey lenders in the shifting of loans. The glucose
stocks were notably affected, the common drop
ping nearly 8 points ar.d sing near the low
est, while the preferred lost 3%.
The Iron and steel stocks as a group also
weakened simultaneously. Steel and Wire lead
ing with an extreme decline of 2%. Others
of the group Buffered to the extent of between
1 and 2 points. This unsettled the bull move
ment and wiped out pretty much all the gains
which had been established. Prices struggled
upward In the final dealings to a level lor the
most part slightly above last night, but the
sap was taken out of the movement. There
was a warning rally in the rates for foreign
exchange during the day which was unheeded.
Signals of distress were In evidence ln the
German money markets and sterling exchange
fel! sharp at the Continental centers. Includ
ing Paris. Discount rates in London responded
with an advance and exchange rates here, both
on Paris and London, rose* in sympathy. Added
to this the fact that money is still going to
the Interior in considerable volume, and that
Government payments through the sub-treas
ury are again falling off, promising a re
newed drain on the money market on account
of revenue receipts and that applications 'for
anticipated payments for Government Interest
continue on a small scale, and the outlook for
easier money ls not promising. This consid
eration was sufficient to give pause to the
efforts to advance prices.
The bond market was moderately active and
prices Improved. Total sales, par value.
$2,005,000. .*-... -..,,
United States bonds were unchanged In bid
quota!

WHEAT FREIGHTSâ The latest charters are
at 36s 3d for wheat, 353 for wheat and barley
and 39s for barley alone, usual options. These
rates show a firm market. The chartered
wheat fleet in port has a registered tonnage of
61,562, against 16,400 tons on the same date last
year;' disengaged, 2013 tons, against 47,900: on
the way to this port, 204,190 tons, against
155,400.
WHEATâ The 'market has relapsed Into a
very unsatisfactory condition. Liverpool de
clined %. The foreigners showed Indications
Of having bought too freely and are now dis
posed to sell. Advancing freights, declining
cables and Increasing stocks make a discour
aging situation. Chicago reports a very weak
market on liquidation, with the demand chiefly
to fill shorts. Wall street again bought mod
erately, but the principal demand was against
puts. Broomhall cabled that India was buying
frcm Australia direct.
Futures declined, but the spot market was
dull and unchanged.

THE STOCK MARKET.
The only change ln securities on the morn
ing session was an advance ln Gas and Elec
tric to $62 25. Business was quiet.
In the afternoon Gas and Electric was lower
at $61 75. and Hana Plantation declined to
$12 62%.
A quarterly dividend of $1 60 per share was
paid Tuesday by the California Safe Deposit
and Trust Company.
The Contra Costa Water Company is now
paying a monthly dividend of 40c per share.
The Hana Sugar Plantation Company has de
clared a quarterly dividend of 50c per share,
payable November 1.
The Makaweli Sugar Company Is now paying
a monthly dividend of 50c per share.
The Hutchinson Plantation Company will pay
a dividend of 25c per share on the 20th.

Sun, Moon and Tide.
United States Coast and Geodetic Survey-
Times and Heights of High and Low
Waters at Fort Point, entrance to San
Francisco Bay. Published by official au
thority of the Superintendent.
NOTEâ The high and low waters occur at
the city front (Mission-street wharf) about
twenty-flve minutes later than at Fort Point;
the height of tide Is the same at both places.
WEDNESDAY, OCTOBER 18.
Sun rises 6:23
Sun sets 5:27
Moon sets 6:00 a. m.
NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column give* the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occurs. â  The heights
given are In addition to the soum. ngs on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign <â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean nf the lower low waters.

MRS. ROCKHILL
HIDES TO AVOID
THE SHERIFF
Will Not Testify at
Husband's Trial.
- â¦
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
9"S Broadway, Oct. 17.
A remarkable set of circumstances
has been developed in the affairs of
Edward Rockhill and the woman who Is
presumed to be his wife.
The Rockhills have resided in North
Oakland for several ears, and Rock
hill until several weeks ago was em
ployed as a heater at the J udson Iron
Works. One Sunday morning last Au
gust tho couple- engaged in an alterca
tion, during which the woman [aimed
Rockhill had stabbed her in the breast.
Later on the samo day he combat was
renewed in a barn on the premises and
she tired a shotgun at him, part of the
charge lodging in his left shoulder.
Rockhill was arrested, examined an i
held to answes before the Superior Court
on a charge oT attempt to murder.
Th.c case was called for trial to-day in
Judge Hall's court, when Deputy District
Attorney Melvin announced that it had
been impossible for the Sheriff to serve
a subpena on the complaining witness,
Mrs. Theresa Rockhill. The prosecutor
expressed the belief that the woman iii.
not wish to testify against her husband
and was therefore purposely secreting
herself.
Judge Hall noted the fact that Mrs.
Rockhill had visited the prisoner at the
County Jail only last Wednesday, tha
very day the subpena had been issued,
whereas tho information had been tiled
on tho 3d inst. "Where a wife is ex
pected to testify against her husband,"
said the court, "there should be no delay
in securing her attendance. The officers
knew she did not desire to testify against
him in this case and had plenty of time
to get her here." Against the protest of
RockhiU's attorney, Asa V. Mendenhall,
the trial was continued until Friday to
give the officers another chance to serve
the subpena.
Now comes the startling information
from the East that the woman is not
RockhiU's wife. The local authorities
have received letters from RockhiU's for
mer home In Pennsylvania to tho effect
that they were never married; that the
children "at RockhiU's home are by a
wife; he is alleged to have deserted in
that State to elope with his present ac-
There is a girl. Annie Walker, who
has been stopping with the Rock
hills and over whom the couple have
frequently quarreled. She is the niece oj
the present Mrs. Rockhill. and informa
tion received points to an abduction. The
Eastern Mrs. Rockhill is now in com
munication with the local authorities.
GEN. FUNSTON TALKS
OF THE PHILIPPINES
OAKLAND, Oct. 17.â General Funston !
of the Kansas regiment delivered a short j
address to-night at a well-attended meet
ing held under the auspices of the Men's
League at the Fnlon-street Presbyterian
Church; He spoke purely about the re- j
sources of the Philippines ancl declared
the new possessions worth many times j
over the $20 000,000 this Government paid
for them.. On the Island of Palawan; the
asserted, there is timber alone worth $150,- j
000,000, and on the inland of Mindanao j
endless riches in teak, ebony and mahog- ;
any, vast forests of wealth.
"The Philippines," said General Fun- .
ston. "contain the greatest body of un
developed wealth of the world." The â¢
"inherent cussedness of Spanish rule" he
ascribed as the cause that has retarded
ail growth and development of what he
termed "the biggest klondike any nation
ever had.'.'
He spoke of the production of hemp and I
the valuable fiber of the banana plant
that could be grown nowhere else, and
gave many interesting statistics.
To the population, which has been vari
ously estimated at between 5,0u0,000 and
10.000.000, he said might be added the many j
who compose the savage tribes that have .
not yet been heard from. Incidentally i
the speaker refuted the statement made i
by an agent of the St. Andrew's Society
of Albany to the effect hat our army
in the Philippines was composed of 45.- |
000 rakes, gamblers and good-for-noth- :
ings, and he branded him as a liar, grace
fully excusing himself for the use -or such !
a term by declaring it to be the only j
language applicable. Referring to the ;
American press, General Funston said
that the accounts of the military phase j
of the war were very fair and correct. '
AN UNKNOWN MAN
ATTEMPTS SUICIDE
While Despondent He Shoots Himself
Twice and Will JProb
ably JDie.
A shabbily dressed man about 80 years
of age attempted suicide shortly after
midnight last night in a lot on Seventh
street, between Harrison and Bryant, by
shooting himself twice in the mouth. He
â¢was discovered by Officer O'Caliahan and
Bent hurriedly to the Receiving Hospital.
He was In an unconscious condition on
his arrive there, and hough Dr. Dray
did everything in his power to revive him
It was without avail. He will probably
die
On his person was found a pawn ticket
from the Star. loan office, made out to E.
J. Allen. It showed that he had pawned
a watch for $3. A .note addressed to a
woman named Eva, asking her to come
at once to him, and signed "Mrs. Boodle,
Sixth street," was also found in his
pocket. A notebook In which were sev
eral Honolulu addresses and the address,
"M 2 Mission street." gave (he police a
slight clew to his identity, Despondency
is given as the cause of his act.

SNOW'S LETTER
CRITICIZED BY
A COUNCILMAN
Mayor May Return
Eight Thousand.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Oct. 17.
Since the exclusive publication in The
Call a month ngo of the fact that Mayor
Snow when Auditor retained commissions
amounting to several thousand dollars, to
which he believed he was entitled, many
members of the city government have
been making inquiry with a view to see
ing if the Mayor cannot be compelled to
return these commissions.
With the division in the City Council
the opportunity to make capita] out of the
situation was not to be lost, and now
I 'resident Rowe. for a purpose which he
lias not vet divulged, but which is known
to be the bring-' of the matter formally
before the Council, has verilied The Call's
statements and has found that the
amount exceeds JSOOO altogether.
When the .. res were published the
Mayor referred to a letter sent to the
Council in 1835, in which he called that
body's attention to the fact that he in
tended to take advantage of the statute
giving him the right to retain 6 per cent
on all personal property tax collected.
The Mayer's contention -,' as and is hat
the Auditor, being held personally respon
sible for the collection of all personal
property taxes, he should make prepara
tion for possible lawsuits, which the new
system would undoubtedly bring about.
A member of the Council, after read
ing the Mayor's letter, which was pub
lished in The Call, said that accord to
the contents of the Mayor's official ex
planation, he should have returned the
money to the treasury on January 1 of
this year.
"If I read the Mayor's letter aright,"
saici the Councilman, "he says that in
1596 he Intended to retain these commis
sions because of the extra duties imposed
upon him by the statute which took ef
fect that year, 1896. In another portion
of the letter he Bays that it is his inten
tion to retain these commissions until
such time as a new statute shall relieve
the Auditor of these special responsibili
ties. According to another published In
terview with the Mayor, he says that the
present Auditor has no authority to with
hold these commissions, because the new
statute went into effect this year. There
fore, 1 contend that on January 1 the
Mayor, according to his own showing,
should have returned the money.
"There Is still another point in the j
Mayor's explanation which has surprised j
many members of the Council. He says i
thatâ writing in IS96â he intends 'this j
year' to retain such commission, and
says nothing about any previous years. |
Yet on looking at the Auditor's reports
for the last ten years. I find that in 1896, \
without any other authority than this lit- j
ter to the Council, he goes back seven
years and arbitrarily withholds from the ;
personal property taxes of 1896 all the
commissions he thought himself entitled
to for the previous seven years. While '
I do not believe for a moment but what
Mr. Snow thought he was legally entitled
to these commissions, still there are
many of us who think otherwise, and it
is our duty to at least make an effort to
discover whether this large sum. amount-
Ing to $SSOO, belongs to the Auditor or is ;
recoverable by the city."
The Ma; I says that all this talk about
the commissions is the work of politicians.
He declares there can be no contention
about his right to these commissions 'and |
he will welcome a legal settlement of the j
question, although he says he hardly \
thinks his political enemies will give him
this much satisfaction.

to the land of my choice is happiness to
me."

make it warm for certain people who had

A MERRY COMEDY AT
ALCAZAR THIS WEEK
There Is no denying the fact that some |
of the members of the Alcazar Stock
Company are very versatile, and among
them all C. F. Montalne is entitled to
first mors. A man who can play one
week the Baron Hartfeld in "Jim the
Penman" with thai subtlety and strength
of execution which marked Mr. Mon
taine's performance of this difficult char
acter, an.l the next week be equal!) suc
cessful as Jose] Poulard in "The
Masked Ball," Is an artist. The former
i.s the characterization of an aristocratic
swindler, whose Impudence Is so great
that it becomes humorous at times, while j
the latter is a poor fellow over whom a
storm la continually breaking In the,
shape of a wife's wrath. On requires
all the dramatic temperament of a drama
tist, while the other necessitates a keen
judgment of comic situations. Mr. Mon
taine, having been very successful in the i
portrayal of both parts, is deserving of i
much praise for great versatility.
Another actor of many resources is Eu
gene Ormonde, who changed the strong
character part of the detective in "Jim
the Penman" for the juvenile role of Dr.
Paul Blondet in "The Masked Ball." I
While the former character is endowed !
with a good deal of an adopted phleg
matic disposition, which is really laugh
able, and accompanied by certain exhi
bitions of occasional cleverness, the lat
ter is the Impersonation of a husband
very much in love with his wife and very
much excited over a plot instigated by
his wife against him as a punishment for
a certain fib he was guilty of. As Mr.
Ormonde was In both parts simply ad- i
mirable, the reputation of an actor par j
excellence cannot be denied him.
Gertrude Foster is this time playing a I
somewhat Jolly roleâ that of a soubrette, j
as it were. She has a drinking scene,
where she does some legitimate acting.
In order to appreciate this scene fully It!
must be remembered that the tipsync-ss is :
only put on. And in this very fact Miss i
Foster is successful, for it is easy to ob
serve that she acts her part, and one is j
surprised that the husband is not aware
of the Joke. He evidently is so excited i
that he cannot see the plot against him.
The stage management Is, as usual, flaw- i
less. ALFRED METZGER.

FILIPINOS CARRY OFF
DR. SANDER'S DISHES
OAKLAND, Oct. 17.â Dr. Ambrose San
der is not at all well pleased with Fili
pinos as guests in his lodging-house on
Franklin street. He swore out a search
warrant to-day for the recovery of a lot
of cups and saucers, table linen, etc.,
which he claims the troupe of Filipinos
who have been performing here the past
week had packed up at his restaurant,
where he had to herd them owing to lack
of rooming facilities. He alleges that
they propose to carry the articles away,
and the Police Court will Investigate to
morrow.

BARK WILNA BURNED
IN TACOMA HARBOR
Was Loading Lumber and Had Many
Thousands of Feet in Her
Hold.
TACOMA, Oct. 17.â The American bark
tt'llna of 1368 tons, owned in San Fran
cisco, was totally destroyed by fire to
night in Tacoma harbor. About 3
o'clock she was found to be ablaze. Tugs
went out to her, but were unable to ex
tinguish the lire with their small hose.
The ship was cut loose from St. Paul
wharf to prevent her from setting tire
to other vessels. The lire department
wanted her towed up to the wharves,
where it was thought the (ire could be
put out With the lire engines.
Before this could be attempted the
flames had gained such headway that
tugs gave up trying to get hold of her.
She burned all night, with the flames
shooting upward mast high and forming
an illumination witnessed b.v the entire
city.
The Wilna arrived from San Franclsco
two weeks ago and was loading lumber
for Sydney. She was under chart by
Charles Nelson and had over a quarter
million feet of lumber in her hold. The
less is estimated here at about $40,000. The
insurance is unknown.
PRESBYTERIAN SYNOD.
Rev. T. M. Boyd Opens the Session at
Vallejo.
VALLEJO. Oct. 17.â The presbytery of
Benicia convened here to-night. It was
opened with a sermon by Rev. T. M.
Boyd of Point Arena. To-morrow after
noon the members will visit the Hartford,
now in commission at Mare Island, and
to-morrow the presbytery will take the
steamer J. D. Peters to Stockton to at
tend the Synod of California.
The presbytery embraces thirty
ministers, thirty-live churches and 2066
communicants, including all the territory
or. the coast from the Oregon line to San
Francisco. Exercises began to-night at
7:30 o'clock, and the public ls invited to
attend all sessions
KILLED IN A MINE.
SALT LAKE, Utah. Oct. 17.â
pounds of giant powder exploded at the
300-foot level of the Rabbit's Foot mine
near Eureka last night, where six men
were at work, killing James Redford and
Oscar Slett.
The other men were brought to the
surface alive, but almost suffocated by
gas. Paul Porta, a miner at the Grand
Central, was killed by a shot going off
while he was still in the winze.
Married in Jail.
SANTA ROSA, Oct. 17.â Albert Kraus
and Miss Viola Moodey of Healdsburg
were married here this afternoon in the
County Jail. Kraus, who is a solicitor for
a portrait company, was brought to this
city about ten weeks ago from Portland,
where he had been arrested on a warrant
sworn to by Miss Moodey. , The couple
were to have been married some time ago,
but Kraus failed to show up on the day
set for the wedding.

TRAINED ON VENEZUELA
American Warships Prevented a
Bombardment of the Town of
Puerto Cabello.
NEW YORK, Oct. 17.â President An
drade of Venezuela was on board the
Red Line steamship Philadelphia, which
arrived from Puerto Cabello and La
Guayra this morning. The offcers of the
vessel, however, had some stories to tell
about the rebellion in the South Ameri
can Republic. When the Philadelphia
was in the harbor of Puerto Cabello on
October 4 and 5 the rebels swarmed over
the hills back of the town and took pos
session of it. The fort was hold by the
Government troops, who threatened to
tire on the town to drive out the rebels.
The cruiser Detroit was lying in the
harbor close to the American consulate
and the Consul forbade the bombardment
of the town. ---.;> --
"If you open fire on the town I shall
proceed to shell your works,'' was the
message he sent to the commandant, ac
cording to the officers of the Philadelphia.
In order to emphasize the message the
guns of the Detroit were trained on the
Venezuelan fortification. That night an
envoy from President Andrade was sent
from Caracas to Puerto Cabello. where he
had a conference with General Castro.
There was a story at Puerto Cabello
that President Andrade had offered to
leave Venezuela if permitted to sell all
the property he held and take the pro
ceeds out of the country with him. Castro
would not agree to these terms. Chief
Officer M. M. Crockett of the Philadel
phia says the insurrection is holding up
thousands of bags of coffee, which can
not be transported to the seashore and
all ships are leaving port light.

Rush at the Sugar Factory.
SALINAS. Oct. 17.â Some idea of the
rush at the Spreckels sugar factory may
be gained from the knowledge that sev
eral extra freight trains have been com
ing and going daily over the Southern
Pacific tracks for some weeks past. Yes
terday an extra with twelve heavily beet
laden gondola cars arrived in the fore
noon. There were some 360 tons of beets
on the cars, and this is only a small por
tion of the daily receipts. Then the
northbound extra freight takes away
dally from six to seven carloads of sugar
to the refinery At San Francisco. This
output will average about 150 tons daily.
T. Kearns of the Plasterers' Union, Wil
liam Baccess of the Bricklayers'
Union, Sam Ferris of the Painters'
Union, F. W. Wetmore of the Build
ing and Trades Council, E. M. Skain
of the Carmen's Union, F. P. Fury of
the Master Plumbers' Union and A.
D. McKinnon of the Coal Dealers'
Union.
The purpose of the gathering is to
consider and put into action plans for
encouraging the wage-earners to do
all their trading at home and to Insure
the employment of organized labor by
merchants.

"1 told the City Clerk," said Mr.

and earning an additional stipend."
Mr. Miller stated that he had brought the
question up to avoid a lawsuit. "I think
a suit the best," replied Mr. Mackie. "We
want it determined whether 'we have
power as Trustees to run municipal af
fairs. The office-holders seem to be over
riding us. If we have no power I want
to know it and I shall then be in favor
of stepping out, as I have felt like doing
several times already.''
Mr. Shulte moved as an amendment to
the original motion that the salary of the
'. deputy clerk be fixed at $5, and Mr.
Mackie seconded It. The amendment was
carried by the votes of Messrs. Brick,
Mackie and Shulte. Mr. Miller and Mr.
: Forderer voted against the amendment.

NORMAL SCHOOL PUPILS
MUST BE VACCINATED
The Edict Is Very Unpopular, but
There Is No Open Rebellion
Against It.
SAN JOSE, Oct. 17.-The 600 young
ladies attending the Normal School
must submit to vaccination. This is due
to an order recently passed by the State
Board of Health, making it compulsory
for the inmates of all State institutions
to be vaccinated.
In the assembly hall this morning Prin
cipal McNaughton read a letter from the
State Board of Health stating vaccination
must be proceeded with at once. This
caused some pouting among the young
ladies, but no open rebellion, and it is
expected that lame arms will be common
among the students for the next few
weeks.

Jumped From a Window.
SELMA, Oct. 17.â William Gallagher, a
Selma rancher who became insane a few
months ago, jumped from a car window
this morning and was so badly injured
that there is little hope of his recovery.
The insane man was being taken to Ohio
by his brother, M. G. Gallagher. While
the latter was bidding goodby to some
friends the insane man jumped from the
window. A searching party found him
a few hours later groaning by the road
side. Gallagher attempted to kill himself
with a knife a short time ago.
Collection for Stanford.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Oct. 17 â
Dr. Jordan has just received word that a
valuable collection of New Zealand fishes
has been presented to the Stanford mu
seum by S. H. Drew of Wanganni, New
Zealand. The specimens, which are now
on the way, are considered a valuable ad
dition to President Jordan's already large
collection of fishes, coming as they do
from so remote a source. Only one other
donation was made to an American insti
tution, and that was to the National Mu
seum at Washington. D. C.

ST. BRENDAN'S FAIR.
To-night will mark the closing of St.
Brendan's fair, which has been open dur
ing the past fortnight at Fremont and
Harrison streets. Notwithstanding the
fact that the useful and ornamental ar
ticles at the various booths have oeen
selling rapidly, there is still quite a num
ber left and a fine opportunity of acquir
ing them by raffle or purchase at "bar
gain sale prices" is presented. Last night
the parishioners attended in large num
bers and toe-night a bier crowd will wit
ness the winding up of the affairs of the
bazaar, The admission is free, and all
will be mad.- welcome whether they invest
or not. An impromptu musical pro
gramme will be given to-night by the
ladies of the different booths. Father
Nugent, the pastor of the church, an
nounced that several hundred dollars is
still required to pay for the street work
assessment on the church property, out
he ls confident that the deficit will be
made up to-night by the charitable mem
bers of his flock.
YOUTHS' DIRECTORY FAIR.
The members of the Olympic Club were
the main attraction at the Youths' Direc
tory Festival last night, and they pre
sented a number of clever athletic feats.
Tin- programme was as follows: Contest
with foils, 1". Serre and J. H. Horigan:
dueling swords. Professor Tronchet and
E. Onion; Indian club swinging, VV.
Smythe; tumbling. Bob Leando. Four
round boxing exhibitions were given by
T. R. Manning and J. F. McCarthy, F. Ast
and P. McQee, S. Simpson and C. Mil
ward. A Cakewalk by Miss Agnes Curley
and Miss Nellie Bradley concluded the
evening's entertainment.
The farce "Turned Up" was presented
last night at Native Sons' Hall for the
benefit of the directory and netted a hand
some sum for the worthy cause. The dif
ferent characters were well sustained by
clever amateurs, and a large audience was
agreeably entertained. The festival will
continue four nights.

WEST STREET
OPENING WILL
BE EXPENSIVE
Plan Completed for a
Big Scheme.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
93S Broadway, Oct. 17.
The plan for the opening of West
street was completed to-day by the City
Engineer, and it contains enough evi
dence to show that there will be a bitter
contest before this boulevard is openecl
from Twenty-second to Twenty-sixth
streets.
This project originated with Council
man Girard, and has received all pos
sible support up to this point. The Coun
cil readily acknowledged the value of cut
ting this broad thoroughfare clear
through, from the- water front to iho
Berkeley line.
The plan shows that lt will be neces
sary to take several large pieces of land
on which are built many houses of
medium value. The line cuts completely
through eight structures and takes in lhe
land on which are about a dozen more.
The lines run through the land from
Sycamore te Twenty-sixth street, requir
ing 198 feet on one side and 200 feet on the
other. A little corner,- 7 by 14 feet, Is
taken off the corner of the east side of
San Pablo avenue and Twenty-fifth street.
On the opposite side a corner 4â¬ by 39 feet
is cut off Isabella street. South of Isa
bella street 425 feet is required to meet
the existing northerly boundary of West
street. The lines are so run that they
are perfectly straight from the boundaries
of west street south of Twenty-second
to the boundaries of West street north of
Twenty-sixth widening out three feet, as
the southern portion of the street is
77 feet wide while north of Twenty-sixth
is 80 feet wide.
The assessment district Is marked off
from Fifth to Forty-seventh streets, and
embraces one entire block on each side
of West street. This throws the assess
ment on eighty-four blocks of property.
The necessary resolution of intention will
be presented to the Council at its next
meeting.

end F. M. Spencer, in the Hopkins Creek
Tailing Claim, in Goodwin Township. Plumas

NOTICE of meeting of stockholders of The Ris-
don Iron and Locomotive Works, to take into
consideration proposed Increase of capital
stock.
Notice Is hereby given that pursuant to -i
resolution and order of the board of directors
of The Risdon Iron and Locomotive Works, a
corporation organized and existing under and
by virtue of the laws of the State ef Califor-
nia, and having its office and principal place
of business at the city and county of San
Francisco, In said State, unanimously adopted
at a regular meeting of said board held on
the seventh day of August, ln the year one
thousand eight hundred and ninety-nine, a
meeting of the stockholders of said corpora-
tion will be held on Wednesday, the Eight-
eenth iy of October, In the year one thou-
sand < ,-ht hundred and ninety-nine, at the
hour . f two o'clock ln the afternoon of said
d*y. at the office of said corporation, which
Is at the building situate on the easterly cor-
ner of Beale and Howard streets, ln said city
and county of San Franclsco. the same being
the principal place of business of said corpo-
ration, and being the building where the
board of directors of said corporation usually
meets;
That the object of said meeting Is to deter-
mine whether or rot the capital stock of said
corporation shall be increased from one million
dollars, divided Into ten thousand shares of
one hundred dollars each, to a capital stock
cf one million one hundred thousand dollars,
divided Into eleven thousand shares of one
hundred dollars each;
That the amount to which lt Is proposed to
Increase the said capital stock Is one million
one hundred thousand dollars.
By order of the board of directors,
L. R. MEAD.
Secretary of The Risdon Iron and Locomotive
Works.

REFEREE'S SALE IN PARTITION.
On Wednesday, November 1, 1899, at 11:30
a. m under a decree of sale of the Superior
Court of Sacramento County, made October 6,
1819 In a partition suit of the heirs of the late j
CHRISTOPHER STROTHMANN, In which
CAROLINE STODIEK Is plaintiff and
CATHERINE NIEDERQUELLE et al. are
defendants, the following described property
will be sold in one parcel at public auction
to the highest bidder for cash, subject to
confirmation by the court:
"All of Swamp Land Survey No. 267 and the
northerly 67.99 acres of Swamp Land Survey
No 269 of the official surveys of swamp and
overflowed lands of Sacramento County, con-
taining 175.02 acres," being the farm of the
said STROTHMANN. Sale will be made on
said land. . . â¢ . M ..
Immediately after the sale of said land, the
livestock, farming utensils, etc., of said farm
will be sold.
This farm is situate on the Sacramento
River, about 9 miles from Sacramento. 4 miles
below Freeport. It has upon It a house and
a good barn, about 50 acres upland, the re-
mainder lowland f appraised in the Superior
Court of Sacramento In the matater of the
estate of said Strothmann, deceased, at $26,-
-370. Assessed for 1898: Land. $10,000: Improve-
ments, $2000. There are 2777 bearing fruit trees
on the farm and 278 non-bearing.
Further information will be given at the
office of McKUNE & GEORGE. . attorneys for
refi ree, Sacramento, and at the office of the
undersigned. LOEWY & GUTSCH,
Attorneys for heirs, 503 California St., San
Francisco.

of Fan Francisco, State of California, In the
matter of WESTERN BEET SUGAR COM-
PANY, a corporation, upon Its application for
a Judgment of dissolution. Noticeâ Notice Is
hereby given that WESTERN BEET SUGAR
COMPANY, a corporation organized and ex-.
Is ting under and by virtue of the laws of the
State of California, and having its office and
place of business in the city and county of
San Francisco. State of California, has filed
a verified application addressed to the Supe-
rior Court of the city and county of San
Francisco, State of California, praying for
a Judgment of dissolution of said corporation,
and that MONDAY, November 13. 1899, at 10
o'clock a. m., at the courtroom of said. court,
Department No. 2. in the City Hall of the
city and county of San Francisco, have been
appointed as the day. time and place for the
hearing of said application.

hand and affixed the official seal of said Su-
perior Court this 10th day of October. 1599.

GOAT'S MEAT
SUBSTITUTED
TOO MUTTON
Fed to County Hos
pital Inmates.
â  â¦ â
CONTRACTORS ARE ACCUSED
â¦
_
STARTLING DISCOVERY BY IN
SPECTOR WTTZEMANN.
Â«
Eoard of Health Will Take Action
Against Hoffmann & Wonne,
Who Supplied the Con
demned Meat.
The startling discovery was made yes
terday morning by Chief Market In- ]
spector N. F. Witzemann that the in- '
mates of the City and County Hospital :
Were being fed on goat's flesh instead of I
mutton, as the result of the substitution
by the firm of Hoffmann & Woenne, who '
have the contract for furnishing the in- '
stitution with mutton.
Witzemann has been recently appointed
and has proved himself very energetic
In the performance of his duties, and with
that end in view went out to the hospital
to Inspect the meat. As he was passing i
through the kitchen he detected a pc- i
culiar odor and asked the cook what it i
was. The latter replied that it was mut- I
ton. Two legs were cooking in the pan j
nnd hanging close by was another leg
and a side, which Inspector Wltxemann
immediately confiscated, having first as-
BurÂ«-d himself that it was goat's meat In
stead of mutton. Witzemann is of the
opinion that the substitution has been go- :
ing on for a long time and that the con- \
tractors are liable on their bonds.
Witzemann came into town with the j
condemned goat meat and Stopped at the
â¢tore of Hoffmann & Woenne, on Market
Street near Third, and showed Hoffman ,
the meat. He said.
"What do you think of that meat?"
Hoffmann, who of course did not recog
nize tho meat In its dressed condition
os that which he had supplied, but recog
nizing the fact that it was goat's meat.
said;
"Well, the man who did that ought to !
be hoi hipped."
"Well." said Witzemann, "you're that '.
man. for that is the meat you have been ;
furnishing the City and County Hospital '
for mutton."
Hoffmann realized that he was cleverly S
caught, and blurted out:
"I do not know whether it is mutton
or goat. But even if it is goat, ain't it
wholesome?"
Witzemann did not discuss the tier
any further, but reported it to Health
Officer Lawler, who will bring it up at
the next meeting of the board, when some j
action will undoubtedly be taken.
VERDICT OF ACCIDENT
IN THE MEYER CASE
INSULATION WORN OFF THE j
LIVE WIRE.
Deceased Put His Hand Upon the Fa
tal Break and Was Killed In
stantly by the Tremendous
Current.
Coroner Ulll held an Inquest yesterday !
morning upon the body of Arnold Isidore !
Meyer, who was killed by a live wire at j
100T4 Market street October 13. The Jury j
returned a verdict of accident and made '
no comment upon the facts brought out I
by the testimony.
J. A. McGinley, after telling the Jury
how the accident occurred, replied to va
rious questions as to the Insulation of the
wires. He said that he could not say that
any wire In this city was perfectly Insul
ated, although it was possible to insulate
wire perfectly. If the Insulation had been
perfect in this case deceased mid not
have been Injured at all by taking hold
of the live wire that killed him. Bo far as
the witness was concerned ho would not
put his hand on any live wire in this city
no matter how well lt appeared to be In
sulated, but he had often done so when
i bilged by the necessities of his employ
ment as a telephone expert.
Every time a man touched a live wire
he took his life; into his hands. Live wires
are subject to abrasions by rubbing
against buildings and other wires, and the
insulation is often overcome by the ero
sion of the weather. He did not observe
any abrasion on the wire that killed the
oeceased. It was eighteen inches away
from the wall.
George A\ . Swinehard testified that
there was an exposed or worn spot in the
Insulation material of the wire at the
place upon which Meyer laid his hand
There was another bare spot in the samo
wire a few feet away. On the night of the
para.de in honor of the returning Califor
nia regiment his little boy, sitting at the
fame window, put out his hand and
touched the wire, but his hand was im
mediately jerked back by his mother Th->
child's fingers were burned by the current
C. O. Poole, superintendent of the elec
trical department, testified that Swine
hard bad not mentioned anything to him
fcbout the bare spot in the wire.
The jury returned a verdict that the de
ceased had met his death as the result of
an accident.
HOSPITAL EXPENSES
SUBJECT OF INQUIRY
DRUGS THAT WERE PAID FOR
WITH DOUBLE PRICE.
Grand Jury Considers the City and
County Hospital Expenditure
in Relation to Allowance.
The Grand Jury met yesterday after
noon, all but five out of the nineteen
members being present. The charges
brought against the City and County
Hospital officials for exceeding the ex
pense allowance was considered and a
number of witnesses were examined.
Dr. Gustave E. Sussdorf, ex-superin
tendent of the hospital, was questioned
ln regard to the working of the institu
tion. He testified that he did not look
Into the price paid for goods, only to see
whether they were received or not
Dr. Washington Dodge was called, and
he testified that he ..could run the institu
tion on $95,000 a year. When asked
whether there was any chance for swin
dling, he thought there was. and said
much could be saved by having a steward
â who is a competent judge of goods Dr
R. â -'â  Payne and Dr. Hodghead also Rave*
Information concerning the workings of
the hospital. b
The most startling testimony wai that
of William M. Hickman, who was for a
long time druggist at the hospital He
claimed that there was an overcharge of
60 per cent on the extracts and pills- also
that there was no real bid for drugs
The board will meet again to continue
its investigations on Thursday afternoon
at 2 o'clock.
Consideration of the school scandal de
tails of which were lately published by
The Call, was postponed.
Fishermen on the Fight.
Four of the fishmonger fraternity have
got into a serious difficulty with the
State Fish Commission. Last Saturday
Deputy C. A. Vogelsang visited the Union
Fish Market and discovered a box of
salmon in the possession of two fellows
named Oust! and Sposito, a violation of
the law which prohibits the taking or
possessing of these fish until Saturday
night. Mr. Vogelsang 'seized the catch
and was taking it away when he was set
upon by the two. aided by several of their
fellow ' employes. Not being of large
stature he was no match for the half
dozen brawny fishermen, who hurled him
aside and carried off the box of fish.
Yesterday the six were arrested on
charges of having salmon in their pos
session during the closed season and of
assaulting and interfering with an officer
in the discharge of his duty. Bail was
fixed at *200 in each cab*
EMPORIUM MANAGEMENT
ENTERTAINS EMPLOYES
C-_. .HE -hundreds of employes of the Emporium and
Golden Rule Bazaar danced last night to their
hearts' content and the management paid the
( _i piperâ ln this, instance Professor John Mar
quardt's Emporium orchestra. The great doors
to the big establishment were closed to the public at 6
o'clock and opened afterward only to admit the gay com
pany of workers, come to enjoy the hospitality of em
ployers.
The occasion was the second annual ball tendered by
the management to its army of helpers, and as on the
former occasion nothing was left undone to add to the
evening's enjoyment. The great dome, around which
the dance was to move, was brilliantly lighted and the
first and second floors were set off with cut flow-ers and
potted palms and ferns. In the cafe were set scores of
inviting tables, sided over by daintily- clad waitresses.
Above ln the band stand was the dynamo of the dance,
the orchestra.
No prettier place for such a function could be imag
ined. Even with the vast horde that puts i's shoulder to
the wheel in the great establishment there was not the
slightest chance of crowding or overheating or becom
ing uncomfortable. The broad circle of newly waxed
tloor around the dome seemed Ike an Invitation to waltz
forever, and the commodious carpet room held a similar
temptation for those preferring to "dawnce the lawn
cers."
That the employes appreciated the courtesy of the
management was noticeable In the early arrivals and in
the thorough enjoyment manifested all through the
night and just a wee bit into the next work day. And
the young men and women of the Emporium make a
good looking throng. Of course last night they were
looking their best and they were an enjoyable sight to
see. The girls in their dainty and becoming gowns had
no suggestion in their make-ups of that primness which
is supposed i" be requisite for handmaidens of commerce.
They had left that all behind at 6 o'clock in the evening
PERJURY CHARGES AGAINST
BERT A. HERRINGTON
TECHNICAL POINTS REGARDING
ACCEPTANCE OF BONDS.
Preliminary Examination Begins
â¢ Before Judge Graham and De
velops a Peculiar Situation.
The preliminary examination of Bert ...
Herrington, ex-District Attorney of Santa
Clara County, charged with perjury on
the complaint of P. H. Bryant of the
Good Government League of San Jose,
was commenced before Judge Graham
yesterday. The defendant was repre
sented by Attorneys B. E. Cothran and
H. C. Moore of San Jose, ' and W. S.
Barnes. Herrington . was one of the
I sureties on the bonds of Ruby Raymond
of San Jose, and the alleged perjury con
sisted of the fact that he swore he owned
property in Monterey County of the value
of $14,000, free from incumbrance, whereas
it was mortgaged. .
Judge Mogan was the first witness, and
i he testified to the woman Raymond being
in his court and being held to answer. In
cross-examination he said that the
original complaints against the woman,
and on which the warrants were issued
for her arrest and the bonds accepted for
her release, had been withdrawn and new
ones substituted.
Judge Treadwell testified -to accepting
the bonds and that he believed then, as he
! believes now, that they were good. He
i simply asked Herrington if he were a
freeholder and was; worth $5000. He did
; not ask him if the contents of the bonds
were true. Attorney Cothran then moved
that the case be dismissed on the ground
thai Herrington had simply sworn that
jhe was a freeholder and worth $5000,
which was true, and anything else in the
affidavit was Irrelevant and immaterial.
The Judge reserved his decision on the
motion.
Prosecuting Attorney Wentworth said
he could not proceed further, as he was
unable to see the original complaints In
the Ruby Raymond cases, as Sergeant
McDonald, their custodian, was out of the
city. He admitted that If the original
complaints did not state a public offense
had been committed, the case would have
to' be dismissed, as the bonds on which
perjury was alleged were not necessary.
The case was continued til! this morning.

feeling between employers and employed which just such
occasions intensify was manifest on all sides. The man

ure of its guests, and the employes testified by their
looks and actions that it all was appreciated.

RAILROAD MEN
SCRAMBLE FOR
SUTRO'S LINE
Sold to the Sutter-
Street Company.
REASONABLE FIGURE IS PAID
STETSON MADE THE BIDDING
EXTREMELY LIVELY.
Adolph Sutro's railroad, the machine
that brought the Market-street Railway
Company to Its knees and reduced all the
fares to the beach to 5 cents, -has passed
Into the hands of the Sutter-street Rail
way Company. Yesterday morning the
Sutro road was placed on sale in Judge
Coffey's court. After spirited bidding for
half an hour R. F. Morrow, president .of
the Sutter-street road, triumphed, and
the sale of the property was confirmed to
him for thefsum of $215,000. About $116,000
of this amount will be used for the pur
pose of liquidating the debts of the Sutro
road, however, which * were assumed .by
Mr. Morrow by express agreement When
he purchased the road.
When the matter of the sale of the road
was taken up by Judge Coffey yesterday
morning President J. B. Stetson of the
California-street Railway Company put In
a bid 0f. 5178,000, an increase of $19,000 over
the original bid made by the Sutter-street
company. Attorney Friedenrich, who
represented Mr. Morrow, objected to the
bid on the ground that it was not made
in good faith, and' Intimated that al
though Mr. Stetson was the president of
the California-street "company, he was
bidding for the road on behalf of the
Market-street company. Testimony was
then introduced with the object in view
of showing that the interests of the es
tate would suffer if the road fell into the
hands of the Market-street company.
Thomas Magee, the well-known real es
tate agent, said that in his opinion an op
position line to the beach should be main
tained if the interests of the Sutro estate
were to be considered, and said
that he believed the estate would profit
if the Sutter-street company came into
possession of the road on a bid $50,000
below that offered by any other company.
E. B. Pond testified that he offered the
road to Mr." Stetson, but that he refused
to bid. Hence the, belief that he repre
sented the Market-street company during
the" sale proceedings ln court. After Mrs.
Merritt had testified that in her opinion
the California-street company had re
fused transfer connections during the
building of the road, Mr. Stetson was
called to the stand. He confessed that
he had once asked H. E. Huntington why
he did not buy the road, and had also
conferred with Superintendent Lynch of
the Market-street company. Mr. Stetson
then lost his temper. He said that he had
made a bid for the road and was prepared
to pay the amount offered and saw no
reason why he should be catechised. .
R. H. Lloyd, who represented the ex
ecu tors of the Sutro estate, then said that
all he desired was to protect the interests
or the estate, and then the court ordered
the sale to proceed.
Attorney Frledenrich then objected to
Mr. Stetson's bid on the ground that It
was made too late, but the court held
otherwise. "Mr. Stetson's bid is 1178.000."
said Judge Coffey, "do I hear any other
$â¢ nnn n^V " riede " r,t * raised the bid to
JIMJ.iiOO, and by spurts the amount offered
W l nt iÂ« u i ) to Â«205.000. Here Mr. Stetson
asked for a continuance that he might
consult his confreres, but it was objected
to. Mr. Stetson then offered $206,000. and
the opposing bidder went $4000 better
Mr. Stetson raised the bid $1000 and then
demanded further time. President Mor
mir f â¢ C^ r 3? re S entatlve then .lumped to
$^10.000 and the battle was won. Stetson
hurried to a phone, held a short con
ference with his backers, and. returning
into court announced that he had no
further bid to offer. Judge Coffey then
confirmed the sale of the road for $215 -
TOO and it passed Into the hands of the
Sutter-street company.

REGENTS HAND
IN HAND WITH
THE PRESIDENT
Wheeler Sits With the
Board.
â â *
MAKES HIS OFFICIAL BOW
â¢
HIS RECOMMENDATIONS FIND
IMMEDIATE FAVOR.
*
Many Changes in the Medical Col
lege â Wilmerdlng School In
structors Named â Inaug
ural Exercises.
At their very first official opportunity
since the arrival of President Ben
jamin Ide Wheeler to take charge
of the University of California the
Regents hastened to do everything
possible to assure him that they
meant what they said when they told him
that he would have their fullest assist
ance and sympathy in his new office. At
the meeting of the board yesterday after
noon at the Hopkins Institute he had only
to offer a suggestion to have the board
vote unanimously in its favor. He made
numerous recommendations for the reor
ganization of the medical college. They
were joyfully adopted. He asked for a
private secretary to help lighten the presi
dential burdens. The board empowered
him to employ one at a salary of $1000 a
year. He made several recommendations
for the appointment of instructors at the
Wilmerdlng School and they were con
curred in, despite the fact that the pro
fessor of blacksmithing will get a larger
salary than the instructor in arithmetic
and trigonometry. Slight changes recom
mended in title- and salary in other de
partments of the university were also

In return for all this nice treatment
President Wheeler was generousness per
sonified. He announced his intention of
reviving the lnstructorshlp in ancient his
tory, now unfilled, in order to do this, he
said, he would assume the role of instruc
tor for the second half of this college
year and recommended that the board se
lect for the position, beginning July 1.
1900, with a salary of $1000, William Scott
Ferguson, Ph.D., now in Europe. The
recommendation was adopted.
The meeting was called for the purpose
of considering the formal inauguration of
Presidenl Wheeler. The committee to
which the arrangements had been referred
reported, recommending as speakers for
the occasion President Daniel C. Oilman
of Johns Hopkins University, President
David Starr Jordan of Stanford and Re
gent A. S. Hallidie. The inauguration ex
ercises will take place on the campus at
Berkeley October 26, beginning at - p. m.
The sum of $700 was voted to defray the
expenses of the Inauguration.
President and Mrs. Gilman will arrive
hece on the evening of October 23, and
after participating in the inaugural exer
cises at Berkeley will be the guests of
Mrs. Hearst at Pleasanton, October 27.
They will remain in California two weeks.
It was moved at the meeting that a recep
tion be tendered them at the Hopkins In
stitute. The chair appointed Regents
Denicke and Hellman and President
Wheeler on a committee to arrange a
suitable date.
The Regents also accepted an Invitation
extended on behalf of the Alumni Asso
ciation by Dr. d'Ancona, W. A. Beatty,
George Edwards and Herbert Dam to
take part in a banquet at the Palace Ho
tel to President Wheeler on the night of
his inauguration. A banquet in honor of
Mrs. Wheeler will be given the same
evening by the wives of the Regents and
the j'oiing ladies of the university.
The finance committee's report recom
mending an additional appropriation of
$6500 tor the enlargement of the Harmon
Gymnasium, making the total $10,000, was
carried. Regents Hearst an.; Hallidie br
ing excused from voting. Regent Denicke
explained before the vote was taken that
the gymnasium, to be enlarged as it
should be to accommodate commencement
day crowds, would cost $20,000. Under the
appropriation as increased it will only seat
2100.

RUSSIAN EXILE HAS
FEARS FOR HIS LIFE
Paul Malnicoff Troubled by a Noc
turnal Visitor in His Residence
on Natoma Street.
Paul Malnicoff, a Russian, who was
banished from his native land to Siberia
about twelve years ago, and who later
escaped to San Francisco, has fears for
his life. He claims that some mysteri
| ous nocturnal visitor, whom he suspects I
jof being a subject of the Czar, climbs :
j over his roof nightly with the Intention of j
assassinating him when the opportunity j
! presents Itself. The trespassing stranger, j
| he says, carries a dark lantern, the rays
j of which he sends through the skylight i
! and into the room occupied by Malnicoff j
I and his wife.
i Twelve years ago Malnicoff was a stu
, dent in one of the Russian colleges. He j
' exercised the right of free speech and in- i
I curred the displeasure of the Russian
j ruler. His exile to Siberia followed and i
; he remained there until four years ago,
when he made his escape and came to ]
this City. 77 7 :â â¢ â ;:â : |
Since his arrival here his peace of mind \
has been disturbed by several queer oc
currences. On one occasion he became
suspicious of food which was served to j
: him in a certain house and refused to eat
it. Instead he fed the stuff to two dogs,
i both ot which died a short while after
i ward. Since he has been residing at his
present address, lOOl'/s Natoma street, noc
turnal visits have distressed Malnicoff to
such^n extent that he now sleeps with a
rifle at the head of his bed and threat
ens to shoot the intruder when he again
makes his appearance. The exile believes
I that some one, loyal to the Czar, who has
discovered his identity, Intends making a
I target of him to satisfy Russian justice.
Booming Our Products.
At the quarterly meeting of the Cham
ber of Commerce yesterday Captain W.
L. Merry, late secretary of the chamber
and now Minister to j Guatemala, was
made an honorary member, of the body.
The chamber indorsed the movement of
the New York Merchants' Association in
the promotion of the project to establish
a permanent exhibit % of all American
products in the Eastern city. A com
mittee will be appointed to assist in carry
ing out the scheme, the purpose being to
boom the products of this State among
those from other States. yyyy
Beaten by Soldiers.
William Harrington, employed on the
', transport Newport, quarreled with three
i soldiers yesterday morning at Powell and
Ellis streets, and during the fight one of
the soldiers bit a piece out of Harring
ton's left ear. He went to the Receiving
Hospital. He and the soldiers were
drunk.
